Last comment 2026-06-03 Re: [PATCH v2 2/3] btrfs: fix incorrect buffered IO fallback for append direct writes Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a potential issue in Btrfs where buffered IO fallback for append direct writes could lead to incorrect i_size updates, causing readers to block on an out-of-evaluation (OE) error and potentially return zeroes. The problem arises from the fact that the invalid i_size is only fixed up within the dio thread holding the inode lock, but released before the extent lock is acquired. Boris Burkov suggests this could lead to a reader observing the intermediate too-big i_size before it's corrected. The patch aims to clarify and fix this potential issue.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Qu Wenruo ↳ replying to Boris Burkov Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the current patch does not address a potential race condition between buffered reads and direct writes, where a buffered reader could observe an intermediate too-big i_size before it is fixed, leading to incorrect behavior. Reviewer noted that all i_size_write() calls are under the extent lock, and suggested keeping EXTENT_DIO_LOCKED hold until iomap_end() to properly update isize
Show original comment 
Buffered read doesn't take inode lock at all, thus inode lock doesn't 
seem enough.

Although I'm not sure if it's a good idea to mix buffered read with 
direct IO in the real world.

---

I just checked all i_size_write() calls inside btrfs, it looks like they 
are all under extent lock.

So even just for the sake of consistency we should already follow the 
existing requirement.


I'll try to explore some methods to properly update the isize.

My current idea is to keep the EXTENT_DIO_LOCKED hold until iomap_end(), 
so that we can properly update the isize inside iomap_end().
But I'm not sure if it's safe or we may have some other hidden pitfalls.

Thanks,
Qu

Last comment 2026-06-05 [PATCH] accept an empty attribute argument list Neutral LLM

The patch proposes to accept an empty attribute argument list in the Linux kernel, aligning with GCC and Clang behavior. This involves modifying the parsing of attributes to handle cases where no arguments are provided.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Dan Carpenter Tested-by Positive LLM
Acknowledged the patch and added Tested-by tag, indicating it works for them but no technical review was provided.
Show original comment 
On Fri, Jun 05, 2026 at 11:30:45AM +0000, Dmitry Ilvokhin wrote:
> GCC and clang accept an empty argument list for attributes, e.g.
> __attribute__((nonnull())), and treat it the same as the bare
> __attribute__((nonnull)). sparse instead rejects it with
> "an expression is expected before ')'".
> 
> ignore_attribute() and recover_unknown_attribute() consume the optional
> argument list with parens_expression(), which requires a non-empty
> expression, so an empty () is rejected. The bare form is fine because it
> takes no parentheses and skips that path.
> 
> This turned up in the Linux kernel: the cleanup.h guard macros expand
> __nonnull_args() to __attribute__((__nonnull__())).
> 
>   https://lore.kernel.org/all/aiJi0WcYE8FZt-jO@stanley.mountain/
> 
> Reported-by: Dan Carpenter <error27@gmail.com>
> Signed-off-by: Dmitry Ilvokhin <d@ilvokhin.com>
> ---
>  parse.c                              | 17 +++++++++++++----
>  validation/attribute-nonnull-empty.c | 12 ++++++++++++
>  2 files changed, 25 insertions(+), 4 deletions(-)
>  create mode 100644 validation/attribute-nonnull-empty.c

Thanks so much!

regards,
dan carpenter

Last comment 2026-06-04 [PATCH v7 0/3] locking: contended_release tracepoint instrumentation Neutral LLM

This patch series introduces a new tracepoint, contended_release, which fires on the lock holder's side when a lock is released while there are waiters. This provides hold time estimation and allows for capturing the holder's stack at release time.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Usama Arif Acked-by Positive LLM
Acked the patch series without any objections or suggestions.
Show original comment 
On Thu,  4 Jun 2026 07:15:07 +0000 Dmitry Ilvokhin <d@ilvokhin.com> wrote:

> Add the contended_release trace event. This tracepoint fires on the
> holder side when a contended lock is released, complementing the
> existing contention_begin/contention_end tracepoints which fire on the
> waiter side.
> 
> This enables correlating lock hold time under contention with waiter
> events by lock address.
> 
> Add trace_contended_release()/trace_call__contended_release() calls to
> the slowpath unlock paths of sleepable locks: mutex, rtmutex, semaphore,
> rwsem, percpu-rwsem, and RT-specific rwbase locks.
> 
> Where possible, trace_contended_release() fires before the lock is
> released and before the waiter is woken. For some lock types, the
> tracepoint fires after the release but before the wake. Making the
> placement consistent across all lock types is not worth the added
> complexity.
> 
> For reader/writer locks, the tracepoint fires for every reader releasing
> while a writer is waiting, not only for the last reader.
> 
> Signed-off-by: Dmitry Ilvokhin <d@ilvokhin.com>
> Acked-by: Paul E. McKenney <paulmck@kernel.org>

Acked-by: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>
[PATCH v7 1/3] tracing/lock: Remove unnecessary linux/sched.h include Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch removes an unnecessary include statement in the lock tracing header file, as none of the trace events reference anything from linux/sched.h.

[PATCH v7 2/3] locking/percpu-rwsem: Extract __percpu_up_read() Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch extracts the slowpath of percpu_up_read() into a new function __percpu_up_read(). The original function is simplified, and the slowpath is moved to a separate function to avoid increasing binary size due to the addition of a tracepoint. The new function is exported as a symbol, allowing it to be used elsewhere in the kernel.

Last comment 2026-06-04 [PATCH v7 3/3] locking: Add contended_release tracepoint to sleepable locks Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a new tracepoint called contended_release to the Linux kernel's locking mechanism. It fires when a lock is released under contention, allowing for correlation of lock hold time with waiter events by lock address. The patch modifies the slowpath unlock paths of various sleepable locks, including mutexes, rtmutexes, semaphores, and rwsems, to enable this tracepoint. The placement of the tracepoint varies depending on the lock type, but it generally fires before the lock is released or after the release but before the wake. This patch also adds support for reader/writer locks, firing the tracepoint for every reader releasing while a writer is waiting.

[PATCH v5 1/4] nvdimm: Convert nvdimm_bus guard to class Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch converts the nvdimm_bus guard from using DEFINE_GUARD() to a class-based implementation, which will eventually require non-null arguments for constructors. The change is made to accommodate this future requirement without introducing functional changes or breaking existing code that passes NULL to the nvdimm_bus lock/unlock functions.

Last comment 2026-06-02 [PATCH v5 2/4] genirq: Move NULL check into irqdesc_lock guard unlock expression Neutral LLM

This patch moves a NULL check from the __DEFINE_UNLOCK_GUARD() macro to the irqdesc_lock unlock expression, making the handling of NULL locks explicit at the call site. This change is in preparation for removing the NULL check from the macro itself. The patch has no functional changes.

Last comment 2026-06-02 [PATCH v5 3/4] cleanup: Annotate guard constructors with nonnull Neutral LLM

This patch series aims to annotate guard constructors in the Linux kernel with nonnull attributes, ensuring that the compiler warns when NULL is statically known to be passed. The approach involves re-declaring the constructor with __nonnull_args() and annotating it directly. This change follows the existing convention for attribute wrappers and avoids clashing with glibc's __nonnull() when kernel and userspace headers are combined.

[PATCH v5 4/4] cleanup: Remove NULL check from unconditional guards Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch removes unnecessary NULL checks from unconditional lock guards in the Linux kernel. The checks were dead code because the locks are guaranteed to be non-NULL at destructor time due to the way the guards are constructed and used. The patch updates several macros, including DEFINE_GUARD, DEFINE_LOCK_GUARD_1, and DEFINE_CLASS, to remove these unnecessary checks. This change is expected to have a small performance improvement.

On Wed, Jun 03, 2026 at 02:17:27PM +0000, Dmitry Ilvokhin wrote:

> 
> I like the static_call idea. It's truly zero cost on x86 (and, as you
> note, even a byte smaller). The one caveat is that it relies on
> HAVE_STATIC_CALL_INLINE to stay free. 
> 
> So my plan would be: static_call where HAVE_STATIC_CALL_INLINE is
> available (x86), and a static branch fallback elsewhere, gated behind a
> default-off config so it imposes nothing on arches/kernels that don't
> opt in. I'm mostly interested in x86, but would like arm64 to work too,
> which would use the fallback.

(i386 doesn't have STATIC_CALL_INLINE, but nobody cares about the
performance on that target, so anything goes really ;-)

> 
> Concretely:
> 
> 1. Split the sleepable-lock patches out and send them separately.
>    They're independent of the static call work and look far less
>    controversial.
> 
> 2. Convert the paravirt spinlock unlock to a static_call, as the
>    foundation for the unlock tracepoint. I'm happy to take a stab at it.
>    Let me know if you'd rather do it yourself.

Yeah, I think that patch as-is *should* work, but like said, I haven't
even tried it, so it could be terribly broken :-)

> 3. Build the unlock tracepoint on top: static_call where it's cheap,
>    config-gated static_branch fallback where it isn't.

Right, so I think we need some sort of custom callback for tracepoint
enable/disable. Its been a minute since I dug through the tracepoint
code, but I don't think it provides that with a convenient wrapper, but
it should be doable.

One thing to note is that when you set the tracepoint unlock function,
it should either tail-call into the original function, or you have to
create two unlock_trace functions, one for native and one for paravirt
and pick the right one.

> Does this plan sound reasonable to you?

Yeah, should work.

> > Also, I think someone should go do some performance runs with
> > ARCH_INLINE_SPIN_* set for x86 just like for s390.
>

Thanks!

[PATCH v4 0/9] dax/kmem: atomic whole-device hotplug via sysfs 9 patches
Show 9 individual patches
  • [1/9] mm/memory: add memory_block_aligned_range() helper
  • [2/9] mm/memory_hotplug: pass online_type to online_memory_block() via arg
  • [3/9] mm/memory_hotplug: export mhp_get_default_online_type
  • [4/9] mm/memory_hotplug: add __add_memory_driver_managed() with online_type arg
  • [5/9] mm/memory_hotplug: add multi-range hotunplug
  • [6/9] dax: plumb hotplug online_type through dax
  • [7/9] dax/kmem: extract hotplug/hotremove helper functions
  • [8/9] dax/kmem: add sysfs interface for atomic hotplug
  • [9/9] selftests/dax: add dax/kmem hotplug sysfs regression test
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series adds atomic whole-device hotplug control via sysfs, allowing for transitions to be made atomically across entire device regions. The existing per-block toggling pattern is preserved for backwards compatibility but deprecated in favor of the new atomic approach. The patch introduces a 'hotplug' attribute and updates mm and dax plumbing to support it, including helpers for online_type and offline_and_remove_memory_ranges. This addresses a race condition between offlining and removing memory blocks, which was discussed at the LPC2025 device memory sessions.

Last comment 2026-06-04 Re: [LSF/MM/BPF TOPIC][RFC PATCH v4 00/27] Private Memory Nodes (w/ Compressed RAM) Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch enables access to private memory nodes through the /dev/xxx pattern, which is a simpler approach than implementing mbind support. The author believes that full mbind support requires more development time and introduces complex interactions with page allocation, migration, cgroups/cpusets, and UAPI. The patch focuses on enabling single-node bind for private nodes, which is considered the most feasible mempolicy to apply.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Balbir Singh ↳ replying to Gregory Price Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer questioned the inclusion of regular memory (N_MEMORY) in the fallback list of device private nodes, assuming ATS translation is enabled and asking for clarification on whether A and B are N_MEMORY or not. Reviewer noted that controlling fallbacks for N_MEMORY_PRIVATE in CDM is similar to the current patch, but highlighted a design decision that needs consideration. Reviewer Balbir Singh noted that reclaim support is necessary for private memory, allowing it to be overcommitted like regular memory, and suggested discussing this further. Reviewer Balbir Singh expressed concerns that introducing full mempolicy support before enabling mbind access to private memory nodes is premature, citing complexity in interactions with migration, cgroup/cpuset, and UAPI. Reviewer questioned whether to use a single-node bind or multi-private-node masks for mbind support, citing potential issues with migration, cgroup/cpuset interactions, and UAPI semantic changes. Reviewer Balbir Singh noted that supporting full mempolicy and task policy on private nodes is out of scope for the initial iteration, as it would require significant changes to migration, cgroup/cpuset, and UAPI interactions, and suggested focusing on mbind support first.

Last comment 2026-06-04 Re: [LSF/MM/BPF TOPIC][RFC PATCH v4 00/27] Private Memory Nodes (w/ Compressed RAM) Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch introduces a new flag, __GFP_PRIVATE, which allows allocations to only access private memory nodes if the flag is provided. If not, it falls back to other nodes. This change also affects how pagecache pages are associated with VMAs and how file-backed allocation works in relation to mempolicies.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Balbir Singh ↳ replying to Gregory Price Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Balbir Singh noted that __GFP_PRIVATE might be too broad, as it doesn't account for the possibility of heterogeneous memory nodes, similar to how not all ZONE_DEVICE memory is homogeneous. Reviewer Balbir Singh agrees with the current design but suggests that __GFP_PRIVATE should be an evolution of the current design, implying a need for further development and refinement. Reviewer Balbir Singh split the discussion into two parts: unmapped page cache should not be impacted by mempolicy and shared pages' mempolicy would need to be on a set of nodes backed by private memory, depending on mbind() policy Reviewer Balbir Singh expressed concern that unmapped page cache pages should not be allocated from or migrated to private memory nodes, requiring further consideration of this aspect. Reviewer Balbir Singh expressed openness to the patch, but initially approached it from a different angle by suggesting mimicking N_MEMORY with N_MEMORY_PRIVATE and selectively modifying features. Reviewer noted that file-backed pages are global resources and can be mapped into multiple VMAs, which could lead to incorrect node assignment if a process maps a file-backed page into private memory on one node and another process maps the same page into private memory on a different node.
Show original comment 
My concern is that __GFP_PRIVATE is too wide, I wonder if we'll have a
need to support N_MEMORY_PRIVATE may not be all homogeneous memory nodes.
Very similar to how not all ZONE_DEVICE memory is homogenous.

Let's split this into two:

   end up on private memory nodes

   be on a set of nodes backed by private memory, depending on mbind()
   policy

Gregory Price (author) ↳ replying to Balbir Singh Needs Work LLM
Author is asking for clarification on reviewer's definition of 'homogeneous' in the context of private memory nodes, indicating a need to understand the feedback before responding. Author acknowledges that if they cannot guarantee isolation without __GFP_PRIVATE, the baseline patch will not be merged and suggests that this flag is necessary for private memory nodes. The author addressed Balbir Singh's concern about the interaction between file-backed VMAs and private memory nodes, explaining that silent migration can occur when a file is cached as unmapped page cache and then mapped at a later time. The author acknowledged that this issue needs to be considered in the design of mbind() support for private memory nodes. The author addressed Balbir's concern that the private memory node design should mimic N_MEMORY, which allows normal memory handling. The author disagreed and explained that their design is 'closed by default', whereas N_MEMORY is 'open by default', to make reasoning about these nodes feasible. The author is addressing concerns about the abstraction of memory allocation for various types of accelerators and special memory, such as network accelerators, GPUs, slow distributed memory, and compressed memory. The author explains that in most cases, a NUMA node is the right abstraction to reason about where memory should come from, allowing devices to be given private nodes to manage their own memory without worrying about general kernel operations. Author acknowledges that the patch is complex and needs to be broken down into smaller features, such as reclaim and mempolicy, before further review can proceed.
Show original comment 
Can you more precise about your definition of homogeneous here?

Are you saying not all memory on a private node will be homogeneous?
   While possible, I would argue that you should not do this and
   should instead prefer to use multiple nodes - 1 per memory class.

Are you saying not all private nodes will be homogenous?
   I don't see the issue with this.

---

Possibly.  If we can't guarantee isolation without __GFP_PRIVATE, then
we probably can't merge the baseline without it.

---

This does not fully describe the problem.

A file can be opened and cached as unmapped page cache, and then mapped
at a later time - at which point the mapped copy would share the filemap
page cache page.

Worse, because it's file-backed, you can have the memory faulted onto
your remote node - reclaimed - and the faulted back in via the process
accessing the file via unmapped operations (read/write), at which point
you've had a silent migration occur.

Basically consider

Process A:
   fd = open("myfile", ..., RO);
   read(fd, ...);  /* mm/filemap.c fills page cache */

Process B:
   fd = open("myfile", ...);
   mem = mmap(fd, ...);
   mbind(mem, ..., private_node);
   for page in mem:
       int tmp = mem[page]; /* fault into vma */

The result of Process A running first is Process B thinks it has faulted
the memory onto private_node, but in reality it's taking soft faults and
just getting the filemap folio mapped in.

If you wanted mbind() support from the start, we would have to limit
applicability to anon memory only.

Shared anon memory is different, as there is a radix tree that deals
with a shared mempolicy state.

---

N_MEMORY essentially states:
	"This is normal memory touch it however you like"

N_MEMORY_PRIVATE (_MANAGED, w/e) says
	"This is NOT normal memory, there are special rules here"

So, no, lets not mimic N_MEMORY.  This is a "closed by default" design,
while N_MEMORY is an "open by default" design.  This design choice is
explicit to make reasoning about these nodes feasible.

---

So far i have collected:

- Network accelerators carrying their own memory for message buffers
- GPUs with semi-general-purpose working memory across coherent links
- Acceptionally slow distributed memory that you do not want fallback
  allocations to (so you want to deliberately tier what lands there)
- Compressed memory (just another form of accelerator really) which
  has *special access rules* (i.e. writes need to be controlled)

In most if not all of these cases, the right abstraction to reason about
where memory *should come from* IS a NUMA node.

- the network stack can be taught to check if the target device has a
  node with memory and prefer that node over local memory

- accelerators can be given private nodes to manage memory using
  core mm/ components, without worrying that general kernel operation
  will put unrelated memory on those nodes or do things like migrate
  your pages out from under you (unless your driver/service requested
  that).

the tiering application should be somewhat obvious / trivial.

---

Really I want to get the minimized set out the door so we can start
breaking this up by feature (reclaim, mempolicy, etc), because trying to
reason about it as a whole is infeasible - and I cannot be the single
arbiter of every use case (I simply do not have sufficient context).

I'm reworking it all as we speak.

~Gregory

Re: [PATCH v4 7/9] dax/kmem: extract hotplug/hotremove helper functions Neutral LLM

This patch series adds atomic whole-device hotplug control via sysfs, allowing for transitions to be made atomically across entire device regions. The existing per-block toggling pattern is preserved for backwards compatibility but deprecated in favor of the new atomic approach. The patch introduces a 'hotplug' attribute and updates mm and dax plumbing to support it, including helpers for online_type and offline_and_remove_memory_ranges. This addresses a race condition between offlining and removing memory blocks, which was discussed at the LPC2025 device memory sessions.

Last comment 2026-06-02 Re: [PATCH] mm: don't allow empty relative nodemask in mpol_relative_nodemask() Neutral LLM

Patch aims to prevent empty relative nodemask in mpol_relative_nodemask() by rebinding mm policy against the effective, online mask (newmems) instead of cs->mems_allowed.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Farhad Alemi ↳ replying to Gregory Price Reviewed-by Positive LLM
Confirmed the bug with a standalone reproducer. Suggested rebinding mm policy against newmems instead of cs->mems_allowed to prevent empty relative nodemask.
Show original comment 
Confirmed, with a standalone reproducer (attached); it panics linus/master
at e8c2f9fdadee. cs->mems_allowed can legitimately be empty
on v2 -- a freshly created cpuset child that never had cpuset.mems
written keeps mems_allowed empty (never initialized) while effective_mems
is inherited non-empty in cpuset_css_online(), and v2 allows attaching
tasks to it (the empty-mems guard in cpuset_can_attach_check() is gated
on !is_in_v2_mode()). So the non-empty guarantee holds for effective_mems,
not for the configured cs->mems_allowed; forbidding empty cpuset.mems
would break v2's inherit-from-parent semantics.

The reproducer enables +cpuset, mkdirs a child without writing
cpuset.mems, moves a task in, mbind()s a VMA with
MPOL_BIND | MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES, and offlines a CPU; the hotplug walk
then calls mpol_rebind_mm(mm, &cs->mems_allowed) with the empty mask and
folds modulo nodes_weight(*rel) == 0 (console logs attached).

The newmems instinct looks right: it's the effective, online mask the
task is actually allowed to use, guarantee_online_mems() keeps it
non-empty, and it matches cpuset_attach(), which already rebinds against
cs->effective_mems. The fix this implies:

  - mpol_rebind_mm(mm, &cs->mems_allowed);
  + mpol_rebind_mm(mm, &newmems);

I built the current base (e8c2f9fdadee) with and without this one-liner:
the unpatched kernel panics on the first cpu1 offline, while the patched
kernel runs the reproducer's 8 offline/online cycles cleanly, with no
divide error.

This regressed in ae1c802382f7 ("cpuset: apply cs->effective_{cpus,mems}",
v3.17), which moved cpuset_attach() to the effective mask but left this
rebind on cs->mems_allowed.

Happy to send this as a proper patch (Fixes: ae1c802382f7, Cc: stable,
reproducer) if you agree the cpuset side is right, or to test a
mempolicy-side fix if not.


Thanks,

Farhad Alemi
PhD Student
SEFCOM Lab @ ASU

Last comment 2026-06-04 [PATCH v4] nfsd: close shrinker/GC/fsnotify vs per-net shutdown race in filecache Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a race condition in the NFS server's file cache, where the shrinker, GC worker, and fsnotify/lease callbacks can unhash an nfsd_file from the rhashtable before it is properly disposed of. The fix involves widening the nfsd_gc_lock to cover the disposal call and adding a spin lock barrier in the per-net shutdown code.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants

Identified the patch as a fix for an existing issue and applied it to the nfsd-testing branch without further comment.

From: Chuck Lever <chuck.lever@oracle.com>

On Thu, 04 Jun 2026 10:31:09 -0400, Jeff Layton wrote:
> The shrinker, GC worker, and fsnotify/lease callbacks can unhash an
> nfsd_file from the rhashtable and then call
> nfsd_file_dispose_list_delayed() to move it to the per-net dispose list.
> If nfsd_file_cache_shutdown_net() runs concurrently, its rhashtable walk
> misses the already-unhashed file, and its drain of the per-net dispose
> list can run before the file has been queued.  The file then sits on
> the per-net list with no thread to drain it, leaking both the file and
> its associated state.

Neutral LLM

This patch series addresses several bugs in the NFS daemon, including a refcount leak and a UAF bug in filecache disposal. The patches simplify the filecache disposal mechanism to prevent these issues. Additionally, one patch fixes a scalability concern by holding net namespace references for nfsd_file objects.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Al Viro ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Neutral LLM
Raised a concern about the scalability implications of holding net namespace references for nfsd_file objects. Suggested that this could lead to cacheline contention on kernels with netns enabled.
Show original comment 
On Mon, Jun 01, 2026 at 01:31:11PM -0400, Jeff Layton wrote:
> Take a net-namespace reference in nfsd_file_alloc() (get_net) and
> release it in nfsd_file_free() (put_net), so that nf_net is always
> valid for files that the GC or shrinker has isolated from the hash
> table and LRU -- which __nfsd_file_cache_purge() cannot see.
> 
> Without this, nf_net can dangle for in-flight files whose net namespace
> is torn down concurrently, causing a use-after-free when
> nfsd_file_dispose_list_delayed() calls net_generic(nf->nf_net, ...).
> 
> Because nfsd_file_free() now calls put_net(nf->nf_net), the old
> nfsd_file_put_local() pattern of returning nf->nf_net after
> nfsd_file_put() is unsafe -- put_net() could theoretically drop the
> last net namespace reference, leaving the returned pointer stale.
> Fix this by moving the nfsd_net_put() call into nfsd_file_put_local()
> itself, before the nfsd_file_put() that may trigger nfsd_file_free().
> The function now returns void and the caller no longer needs to handle
> the net reference.

That means that each nfsd_file_alloc()/nfsd_file_free() is now touching
the same cacheline on kernels with netns enabled.  Scalability implications
might be interesting...
Signals: scalability concerns raised
Jeff Layton Neutral LLM
Acknowledged Al Viro's concern and proposed an alternative solution to only take net references for GC'ed files. Indicated that this would reduce some get/put_net activity, but still might be substantial.
Show original comment 
On Mon, 2026-06-01 at 18:50 +0100, Al Viro wrote:
> That means that each nfsd_file_alloc()/nfsd_file_free() is now touching

Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series addresses several bugs in the NFS daemon that can be triggered by an unprivileged user or require administrative access. The patches simplify the filecache disposal mechanism, fix reference counting issues, and address a use-after-free bug. They also unify cleanup operations in nfsd_cross_mnt() exits and widen address fields to sockaddr_storage. The changes aim to improve the stability and security of the NFS daemon by fixing locally-triggerable bugs.

  • [6/6] nfsd: fix layout fence worker double-reference race Submitted 2026-05-31
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series addresses medium-severity bugs in the NFS server code, including memory leaks and refcounting issues that can be triggered remotely. The patches fix problems such as file handle verification, path reference management, and layout fence worker synchronization. The changes aim to prevent potential buffer overruns and improve overall stability of the NFS server.

Chuck Lever Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that on the v4.0 replay path, op_release is not called unconditionally after every operation, leading to a memory leak of two posix_acl refs, and requested that op->u be released on this branch as well.
Show original comment 
The double-free fix is right, but op_release is not called
unconditionally after every op, and that gap leaks the ACLs on the
v4.0 replay path.

op_release runs only at the release: label inside
nfsd4_encode_operation().  The compound loop skips that encoder on
a replay:

        if (op->status == nfserr_replay_me) {
                nfsd4_encode_replay(resp->xdr, op);     /* no op_release */
                ...
        } else {
                nfsd4_encode_operation(resp, op);       /* op_release here */
        }

So every ACL-bearing v4.0 OPEN retransmit leaks two posix_acl refs.

Please release op->u on the replay branch too:

        if (op->status == nfserr_replay_me) {
                op->replay = &cstate->replay_owner->so_replay;
                nfsd4_encode_replay(resp->xdr, op);
                status = op->status = op->replay->rp_status;
                if (op->opdesc->op_release)
                        op->opdesc->op_release(&op->u);
        }

Let's fix the "unconditionally after every op" wording too.

I've applied the other 5 in this series, so you can just resend
this one.

Last comment 2026-06-01 [PATCH v3] nfsd: release OPEN-decoded posix ACLs via op_release Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a memory leak in the NFS server by releasing posix ACLs allocated for OPEN compounds. The fix involves moving the op_release call to a single location that covers both normal encoding and replay paths, ensuring that resources attached to op->u are properly released.

Re: [brauner-github:vfs-7.2.misc 28/28] fs/open.c:112:29: warning: converting the result of '<<' to a boolean always evaluates to true Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-03 Re: [PATCH v2 8/9] nfsd: hold net namespace reference for delayed-dispose nfsd_files Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a false positive concern about a potential bug in the nfsd code. The issue was that the global shrinker unhashes and isolates an nfsd_file onto a local dispose list, but then the net namespace teardown completes and struct net is freed before the shrinker resumes and calls get_net() on freed memory. However, the reviewer's analysis shows that this scenario cannot happen because the struct net outlives all pernet exit callbacks, including nfsd_net_exit(), and the isolation and get_net() happen in the same execution context. The patch does not change any code, but rather confirms that the existing placement of get_net() is correct and ensures that nf_net remains valid for an arbitrarily long time while the file sits on the per-net dispose list waiting for an nfsd thread to close it.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Chuck Lever ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
The reviewer pointed out two specific technical issues: (1) the patch removes a necessary ordering between nfsd_file_put() and percpu_ref_exit(), which could lead to nfsd_shutdown_net() continuing before the file is freed, and (2) the get_net() placement in nfsd_file_dispose_list_delayed() is incorrect because it touches freed memory. The reviewer suggested keeping the original void API but putting the file ref first, then the net ref.
Show original comment 
That rebuttal answers a different finding. The three-step scenario it
refutes is the one sashiko raised against the get_net() placement in
nfsd_file_dispose_list_delayed(). The review quoted above is about
the put order in nfsd_file_put_local() and does not involve struct
net's refcount at any step. Two new issues appear with 8/9:

1. Put order in nfsd_file_put_local()
  
The question is narrow: after an I/O completion thread executes
nfsd_net_put() -- dropping the last nfsd_net_ref and running
complete(&nn->nfsd_net_free_done) -- what prevents nfsd_shutdown_net()
from continuing through percpu_ref_exit() and nfsd_shutdown_generic()
into nfsd_file_cache_shutdown() before that same thread executes the
nfsd_file_put() on the next line?

In the current code the answer is the ref itself: the caller holds it
across nfsd_file_put(), and percpu_ref runs the release callback only
after every ref drops, so global teardown strictly follows the file
free and the rcu_barrier() in nfsd_file_cache_shutdown() flushes the
call_rcu() that nfsd_file_free() queued. The patch removes that
ordering and installs nothing in its place.

The nfs_uuid handshake does not cover this path: each pgio holds its
own nfsd_file + nfsd_net ref pair (fs/nfs/pagelist.c, stored in
iocb->localio), released at I/O completion through
nfsd_file_put_local(), and an iocb is not on nfs_uuid->files. The
purge has already unhashed the file but cannot free it
(nfsd_file_cond_queue() sees the elevated refcount), so the
completion thread's put is the final one and its nfsd_file_free()
races kmem_cache_destroy(nfsd_file_slab).

The stale-net hazard the commit message cites cannot occur on this
path: NFSD_FILE_NET_HELD is set only via refcount_dec_if_one() in
nfsd_file_lru_cb(), i.e. at refcount == 1, and a file with an
outstanding LOCALIO reference has refcount >= 2. So the fix is to
keep the void API but put the file ref first, then the net ref.

2. get_net() placement in nfsd_file_dispose_list_delayed()

The rebuttal's struct-net argument does not hold for the files in
question: they are no longer on the LRU (nfsd_file_lru_cb() unhashed
and isolated them onto the worker's private list), and an nfsd_file
holds no net reference -- that absence is what this patch is fixing.

The commit message itself states the premise: the purge cannot see
these files and nf_net can dangle. With a second nfsd-serving netns
keeping nfsd_users > 0, nothing quiesces the shrinker or the
laundrette during per-net teardown (cancel_delayed_work_sync() and
shrinker_free() run only in global shutdown).

A worker preempted between isolating a file and calling
dispose_list_delayed() can resume after cleanup_net() has freed the
namespace, at which point get_net(nf->nf_net), net_generic(), and
the fcache_dispose_lock all touch freed memory. The get_net() sits
at the consuming end of the window it is meant to close. v1 took
the reference in nfsd_file_alloc(), the one place guaranteed to run
while the net is alive; the v2 plan of taking it at alloc time for
GC-capable files only would address Al's cacheline concern without
reopening the window.


As a process note, I'll note that I haven't found any non-pre-
existing issues with the other patches on this series.


-- 
Chuck Lever

  • [08/10] fuse: support registered buffers as buffer pools in io-uring Submitted 2026-05-22
  • [09/10] fuse: add zero-copy over io-uring Submitted 2026-05-22
  • [10/10] docs: fuse: add io-uring buffer pool and zero-copy documentation Submitted 2026-05-22
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds buffer pools and zero-copy functionality to the fuse io-uring implementation, allowing servers to pass in a managed buffer pool that reduces memory usage requirements and enables future optimizations. It also allows for direct access to client pages or page cache folios without copying data through an intermediary buffer, requiring CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges. The changes are based on registered buffers and zero-copy, which can provide significant throughput improvements depending on the server's per-request latency and backing I/O characteristics.

0 participants
Joanne Koong (author) Positive LLM
The author is addressing a concern about duplicated logic in fuse_uring_send_next_to_ring() and fuse_uring_send(). They simplified the logic by removing duplicate code from fuse_uring_send_next_to_ring(), decoupling request fetching from the send operation, and reducing unnecessary parameter passing. The patch removes 50 lines of code and adds 28 new lines.

This gets rid of fuse_uring_send_next_to_ring() which contained
duplicated logic from fuse_uring_send(). This decouples request fetching
from the send operation, which makes the control flow clearer and
reduces unnecessary parameter passing.

+static int ring_header_type_offset(enum fuse_uring_header_type type)
 {
+	switch (type) {
+	case FUSE_URING_HEADER_IN_OUT:
+		return 0;
+	case FUSE_URING_HEADER_OP:
+		return offsetof(struct fuse_uring_req_header, op_in);
+	case FUSE_URING_HEADER_RING_ENT:
+		return offsetof(struct fuse_uring_req_header, ring_ent_in_out);
+	default:
+		WARN_ONCE(1, "Invalid header type: %d\n", type);
+		return -EINVAL;
+	}
+}
+
+static int copy_header_to_ring(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+			       enum fuse_uring_header_type type,
+			       const void *header, size_t header_size)
+{
+	int offset = ring_header_type_offset(type);
+	void __user *ring;
+
+	if (offset < 0)
+		return offset;
+
+	ring = (void __user *)ent->headers + offset;
+
 	if (copy_to_user(ring, header, header_size)) {
 		pr_info_ratelimited("Copying header to ring failed.\n");
 		return -EFAULT;

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed, agreed to restructure in v2
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the tight coupling between ents and buffers in fuse io-uring by explaining their new buffer pool design, which decouples them and allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption.
Show original comment 
Right now, ents and buffers are tightly coupled in fuse io-uring where
each entry has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for
N entries where each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the
maximum payload size. This is suboptimal as most request types (lookup,
open, release, getattr, etc) require vastly less bytes than the maximum
payload size and some requests (unlink, rmdir, fsync, flush, etc) do not
require payload buffers at all.

Instead of requiring a 1:1 coupling between ents and payload buffers,
allow the server to pass in a buffer pool (a contiguous allocation of
buffers) that the kernel will use as it wishes for servicing
ents/requests. Buffers are now pooled and selected on demand. Entries
only "reserve" a buffer while actively processing a request that
requires a payload buffer. This decoupling reduces the memory usage
requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring. It allows for future
optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption which will allow
non-overlapping regions of a single buffer to be used simultaneously
across multiple ents/requests, and the possibility of multiple buffer
sizes to be supported per ring in the future.

To use this, the server must set the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag during
registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec
payload in the first sqe. The server must set init.buf_size and
init.nr_bufs in the struct fuse_uring_cmd_req it passes to the kernel to
describe the buffer pool configuration. Buffer pool usage is set on a
per-ring basis. All subsequent registration SQEs for the same ring must
pass in consistent flags.

Internally, a buffer ring data structure is used for managing the buffer
pool. The kernel manages buffer selection and recycling through this
ring structure. A payload buffer is selected from the ring on demand if
the request requires a payload buffer and recycled after the request
completes if used.

The contiguous buffer pool passed in is partitioned into individual
buffers according to the buf_size passed in at registration time.

  PAYLOAD BUFFER POOL (contiguous, provided via iov[1] in first sqe):
    |<-------------- payload_size ------------>|
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+
    |  buf [0]   |  buf [1]  |  buf [2]  | ... |
    |  buf_size  |  buf_size |  buf_size | ... |
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+

The buffer ring logic is as follows:
select:  buf = bufs[head % nbufs]; head++
recycle: bufs[tail % nbufs] = buf; tail++
empty:   tail == head (no buffers available)
full:    tail - head >= nbufs

Buffer ring request flow
------------------------
|  Kernel                                  |  FUSE daemon
|                                          |
|  [client request arrives]                |
|  >fuse_uring_send()                      |
|    [select payload buf from pool]        |
|    >fuse_uring_select_buffer()           |
|    [copy headers to ent]                 |
|    >copy_header_to_ring()                |
|    [copy payload to selected buf]        |
|    >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()            |
|    [set buf_id in ent_in_out header]     |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                  |
|                                          |  [CQE received]
|                                          |  [read headers]
|                                          |  [read payload from buf_id]
|                                          |  [process request]
|                                          |  [write reply to header]
|                                          |  [write reply payload to
|                                          |    buf]
|                                          |  >io_uring_submit()
|                                          |   COMMIT_AND_FETCH
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()              |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                  |
|     [copy reply from ring]               |
|     >fuse_uring_recycle_buffer()         |
|    >fuse_uring_get_next_fuse_req()       |

Reviewed-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+/*
+ * sqe->addr is a ptr to an iovec array, iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_HEADERS] has the
+ * headers, iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD] the payload
+ */
+static int fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(const struct io_uring_sqe *sqe,
+					 struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS])
+{
+	struct iovec __user *uiov = u64_to_user_ptr(READ_ONCE(sqe->addr));
+	struct iov_iter iter;
+	ssize_t ret;
+
+	if (sqe->len != FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/*
+	 * Direction for buffer access will actually be READ and WRITE,
+	 * using write for the import should include READ access as well.
+	 */
+	ret = import_iovec(WRITE, uiov, FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS,
+			   FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS, &iov, &iter);
+	if (ret < 0)
+		return ret;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_bufpool_setup(struct io_uring_cmd *cmd,
+				    struct fuse_ring_queue *queue)
+{
+	const struct fuse_uring_cmd_req *cmd_req =
+		io_uring_sqe128_cmd(cmd->sqe, struct fuse_uring_cmd_req);
+	unsigned int buf_size = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.buf_size);
+	unsigned int nr_bufs = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.nr_bufs);
+	struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS];
+	uintptr_t payload_addr;
+	size_t payload_size, ring_size;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+	unsigned int i;
+	int err;
+
+	if (!buf_size || !nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	err = fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(cmd->sqe, iov);
+	if (err)
+		return err;
+
+	payload_addr = (uintptr_t)iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_base;
+	payload_size = iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_len;
+
+	if (payload_size < (uint64_t)buf_size * nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/* create the pool, which is managed as a ring buffer */
+	ring_size = struct_size(pool, bufs, nr_bufs);
+	pool = kzalloc(ring_size, GFP_KERNEL_ACCOUNT);
+	if (!pool)
+		return -ENOMEM;
+
+	/* populate the ring buffer */
+	for (i = 0; i < nr_bufs; i++, payload_addr += buf_size) {
+		struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf = &pool->bufs[i];
+
+		buf->addr = payload_addr;
+		buf->len = buf_size;
+		buf->id = i;
+	}
+
+	pool->nbufs = nr_bufs;
+	pool->tail = nr_bufs;
+
+	queue->bufpool = pool;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+/*
+ * If the queue is already registered, check that the queue was initialized with
+ * the same init flags set for this FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmd. all
+ * FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmds should have the same init fields set on a
+ * per-queue basis.
+ */
+static bool queue_init_flags_consistent(struct fuse_ring_queue *queue,
+					u64 init_flags)
+{
+	bool bufpool = init_flags & FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL;
+
+	return bufpool_enabled(queue) == bufpool;
+}

+static bool fuse_uring_req_has_payload(struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	struct fuse_args *args = req->args;
+
+	return args->in_numargs > 1 || args->out_numargs;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_select_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool = queue->bufpool;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	unsigned int tail = pool->tail, head = pool->head;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (tail == head)
+		return -ENOBUFS;
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[head % pool->nbufs];
+	pool->head++;
+
+	ent->payload_buf = *buf;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static void fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *ent_payload = &ent->payload_buf;
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(queue) || !ent_payload->addr)
+		return;
+
+	pool = queue->bufpool;
+
+	/* pool should never be full */
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(pool->tail - pool->head >= pool->nbufs);
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[(pool->tail) % pool->nbufs];
+	*buf = *ent_payload;
+
+	pool->tail++;
+
+	memset(ent_payload, 0, sizeof(*ent_payload));
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_next_req_update_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+					     struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	bool buffer_selected;
+	bool has_payload;
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	buffer_selected = !!ent->payload_buf.addr;
+	has_payload = fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req);
+
+	if (has_payload && !buffer_selected)
+		return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+
+	if (!has_payload && buffer_selected)
+		fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(ent);
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_prep_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				  struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	/* no payload to copy, can skip selecting a buffer */
+	if (!fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req))
+		return 0;
+
+	return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+}
+

 				   struct fuse_req *req)

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o request, explaining that registered buffers eliminate this overhead by pinning pages upfront. The author provided details on how to use registered buffers, including setting flags in the registration sqe and following standard protocol for using registered buffers.
Show original comment 
Allow servers to use an io-uring registered buffer as the buffer pool.
When the server registers buffers with io-uring, the pages backing the
buffers are pinned upfront. This eliminates the overhead of
pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o
request. This also allows servers to use the same registered buffers for
subsequent backing store I/O (eg read_fixed/write_fixed), keeping data
in the same pinned pages without additional pinning or mapping overhead
required.

Registered buffers require the buffer pool (FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL) to be
enabled. To use registered buffers, the server needs to register the
payload buffers through io-uring and during registration, set the
FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS init flag (alongside the
FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag) in the registration sqe. For all sqes the
server passes in (including the registration ones), it needs to follow
standard protocol for using registered buffers (eg set
sqe->uring_cmd_flags to include IORING_URING_CMD_FIXED, and pass in the
index where the registered buffers reside to sqe->buf_index).

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput
improvement for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4%
improvement for direct sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s),
a 8% improvement for buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s),
and a 6% improvement for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to
~2670 MiB/s).

Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed, provided explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the zero-copy implementation's complexity and overhead, explaining that it requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS to prevent partially-optimized modes. The author provided detailed request flow diagrams for both write and read paths, highlighting how pages are registered and unregistered in the io_uring registered buffer table.
Show original comment 
Implement zero-copy for fuse io-uring, eliminating memory copies between
userspace, the kernel, and the fuse server for read/write operations.
The server can directly access client pages or page cache folios without
copying data through an intermediary buffer. When a fuse request arrives,
the kernel registers the relevant pages into a sparse slot in the
server's io_uring registered buffer table. The server can then operate
on these pages directly using io-uring fixed buffer operations (eg
read_fixed/write_fixed) and the kernel unregisters these pages when the
request completes. Non-page-backed args (eg op out headers) will go
through the payload buffer as normal.

This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN and FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS (which
requires using bufpools). Gating on registered buffers keeps the
configuration space small and avoids partially-optimized modes that are
unlikely to be useful in practice. This is gated behind CAP_SYS_ADMIN
because zero-copy allows the server direct access to the client's
underlying pages, rather than operating on an intermediary buffer that
the contents of the client's pages were copied into or on page cache
folios.

The request flow for the zero-copy write path (client writes data,
server reads it) is as follows:
=======================================================================
|  Kernel                                   |  FUSE server
|                                           |
|  "write(fd, buf, 1MB)"                    |
|                                           |
|  >sys_write()                             |
|    >fuse_file_write_iter()                |
|      >fuse_send_one()                     |
|        [req->args->in_pages = true]       |
|        [folios hold client write data]    |
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()               |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(IN_OUT)           |
|      [memcpy fuse_in_header]              |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(OP)               |
|      [memcpy write_in header]             |
|                                           |
|    >fuse_uring_args_to_ring()             |
|      >setup_fuse_copy_state()             |
|        [skip_folio_copy = true]           |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()       |
|        [folio_get for each client folio]  |
|        [build bio_vec array from folios]  |
|        >io_buffer_register_bvec()         |
|          [register pages at
                 ent->zero_copy_index]      |
|        [ent->zero_copied = true]          |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_copy_args()                    |
|        [skip_folio_copy => return 0       |
|         for page arg, skip data copy]     |
|                                           |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(RING_ENT)         |
|      [memcpy ent_in_out]                  |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                   |
|                                           |
|                                           | [CQE received]
|                                           |
|                                           | [issue io_uring READ at
|                                           |  ent->zero_copy_index]
|                                           | [reads directly from
|                                           |client's pages (ZERO_COPY)]
|                                           |
|                                           | [write data to backing
|                                           | store]
|                                           |  [submit COMMIT AND FETCH]
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()               |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                   |
|      >fuse_uring_copy_from_ring()         |
|    >fuse_uring_req_end()                  |
|      >io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index) |
|        [unregister sparse buffer]         |
|      >fuse_zero_copy_release()            |
|        [folio_put for each folio]         |
|      [ent->zero_copied = false]           |
|      >fuse_request_end()                  |
|        [wake up client]                   |

The zero-copy read path is analogous.

Some requests may have both page-backed args and non-page-backed args.
For these requests, the page-backed args are zero-copied while the
non-page-backed args are copied to the buffer selected from the buffer
ring:
    zero-copy: pages registered via io_buffer_register_bvec()
    non-page-backed: copied to payload buffer via fuse_copy_args()

For a request whose payload is zero-copied, the
registration/unregistration path looks like:

    register:  fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()
                 folio_get() for each folio
                 io_buffer_register_bvec(ent->zero_copy_index)

    unregister: fuse_uring_req_end()
                  io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index)
                  -> fuse_zero_copy_release() callback
                     folio_put() for each folio

The throughput improvement from zero-copy depends on how much of the
per-request latency is spent on data copying vs backing I/O. When
backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible fraction of
overall latency. Please also note that for the server to read/write
into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
from eliminating the memcpy.

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s).

The benchmarks were run using:
  fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
  --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1

Reviewed-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+static void fuse_zero_copy_release(void *priv)
+{
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs = priv;
+	unsigned int i;
+
+	for (i = 0; i < zc_bvs->nr_bvs; i++)
+		folio_put(page_folio(zc_bvs->bvs[i].bv_page));
+
+	kfree(zc_bvs);
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				       struct fuse_req *req,
+				       unsigned int issue_flags)
+{
+	struct fuse_args_pages *ap;
+	int err, i, ddir = 0;
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs;
+	struct bio_vec *bvs;
+
+	/* out_pages indicates a read, in_pages indicates a write */
+	if (req->args->out_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_DEST;
+	if (req->args->in_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_SOURCE;
+
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(!ddir);
+
+	ap = container_of(req->args, typeof(*ap), args);
+
+	zc_bvs = kmalloc(struct_size(zc_bvs, bvs, ap->num_folios),
+			 GFP_KERNEL_ACCOUNT);
+	if (!zc_bvs)
+		return -ENOMEM;
+
+	zc_bvs->nr_bvs = ap->num_folios;
+	bvs = zc_bvs->bvs;
+	for (i = 0; i < ap->num_folios; i++) {
+		bvs[i].bv_page = folio_page(ap->folios[i], 0);
+		bvs[i].bv_offset = ap->descs[i].offset;
+		bvs[i].bv_len = ap->descs[i].length;
+		folio_get(ap->folios[i]);
+	}
+
+	err = io_buffer_register_bvec(ent->cmd, bvs, ap->num_folios,
+				      fuse_zero_copy_release, zc_bvs,
+				      ddir, ent->zero_copy_index,
+				      issue_flags);
+	if (err) {
+		fuse_zero_copy_release(zc_bvs);
+		return err;
+	}
+
+	ent->zero_copied = true;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+

  */

On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 1:59 PM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> Changelog
> ---------
> v2: https://lore.kernel.org/linux-fsdevel/20260402162840.2989717-1-joannelkoong@gmail.com/
> v2 -> v3:
> * Rework the uapis to be more ergonomic. Use io-uring registered buffers
>   infrastructure instead of doing pinning logic in fuse. Get rid of header
>   pinning as that makes no real perf difference
> * Rename from "buffer ring" to buffer pool. Logic is the same, just different
>   naming

Just to reiterate, the logic is the same between this version and the
previous versions. I just renamed it to "buffer pool" to decouple the
implementation detail from the concept since it's uapi facing. The
buffer pool is managed as a buffer ring internally.

Bernd, it would be much appreciated if you could take a look at this
series when you have the time. I'm targeting the June merge window and
the io-uring dependent changes have already been queued up in Jens's
tree since the last merge window.

Thanks,
Joanne

---

On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 2:51 PM Bernd Schubert <bernd@bsbernd.com> wrote:

> >> v2 -> v3:
> >> * Rework the uapis to be more ergonomic. Use io-uring registered buffers
> >>   infrastructure instead of doing pinning logic in fuse. Get rid of header
> >>   pinning as that makes no real perf difference
> >> * Rename from "buffer ring" to buffer pool. Logic is the same, just different
> >>   naming
>
> short question, I didn't check the code is header pinning still
> *possible*? The problem if we do not pin headers is that
> io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() cannot be avoided, as the application
> cannot write into fuse-server buffers and headers without pre-pinned
> pages. And io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() basically means a context
> switch for all sync requests.

Hi Bernd,

Header pinning was dropped from this patchset. I have a separate
branch that has header pinning alongside the changes for avoiding the
fuse io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() deferral. I moved the header
pinning changes out of this patchset as it doesn't tie in with getting
perf improvements from using registered buffers, unlike payload buffer
pinning.

>
> >
> > Just to reiterate, the logic is the same between this version and the
> > previous versions. I just renamed it to "buffer pool" to decouple the
> > implementation detail from the concept since it's uapi facing. The
> > buffer pool is managed as a buffer ring internally.
> >
> > Bernd, it would be much appreciated if you could take a look at this
> > series when you have the time. I'm targeting the June merge window and
> > the io-uring dependent changes have already been queued up in Jens's
> > tree since the last merge window.
>
> I will try and right is probably a good time, almost through with

Thanks!

> libfuse sync-init (remaining are bugs), Darricks series is also almost
> ready. Although I don't promise this weekend - reserved for Darrick and
> other libfuse changes.
>
> One piece I had been pondering a bit about, though did not dive into the
> code yet.
> What is if payload would be registered per ring entry (as without your
> changes) and just the header would become a buffer ring (so that could

I think payload buffers must be decoupled from ring entries if we want
to reduce memory usage in the non-zero-copy path.

> be pinned that way without loosing one page per entry)?  For zero-copy

For the header pinning changes in my branch, the headers are one
contiguous region (not per-entry), which circumvents having to
allocate one page per entry.

> we could register entries without a payload? Advantage here would be
> that that header buffer would determine max number of entries, only
> little memory would be needed for over-subscribed entries, but entries
> themselves could be registered dynamically on demand.

For zero-copy, entries still may have payloads (though the buffers
will definitely not need to be as big as the max payload size!),
either for the non-zero-copyable args or if the request's args aren't
page-backed. Unfortunately I think many servers may not be able to use
zero-copy due to the CAP_SYS_ADMIN requirements, so I think it'd be
ideal to minimize the memory consumption needed for the non-zero-copy
path, through the buffer pool for the payload buffers.

I hope I'm interpreting your question correctly - if not, please let me know!

Thanks,
Joanne

> Bernd

> Thanks,

View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed Bernd's concern about registering ring entries without payloads, explaining that entries may be used for servicing requests even if there are less buffers than the number of entries, and that it would allow multiple entries to use non-overlapping regions of the same buffer concurrently. The author also mentioned that supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring and some requests not needing payload buffers justify decoupling entries from buffers.

On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 3:49 PM Bernd Schubert <bernd@bsbernd.com> wrote:
>
> On 5/23/26 00:38, Joanne Koong wrote:
> > On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 2:51 PM Bernd Schubert <bernd@bsbernd.com> wrote:
> >>
> >> One piece I had been pondering a bit about, though did not dive into the
> >> code yet.
> >> What is if payload would be registered per ring entry (as without your
> >> changes) and just the header would become a buffer ring (so that could
> >
> > I think payload buffers must be decoupled from ring entries if we want
> > to reduce memory usage in the non-zero-copy path.
> >
> >> be pinned that way without loosing one page per entry)?  For zero-copy
> >
> > For the header pinning changes in my branch, the headers are one
> > contiguous region (not per-entry), which circumvents having to
> > allocate one page per entry.
> >
> >> we could register entries without a payload? Advantage here would be
> >> that that header buffer would determine max number of entries, only
> >> little memory would be needed for over-subscribed entries, but entries
> >> themselves could be registered dynamically on demand.
> >
> > For zero-copy, entries still may have payloads (though the buffers
> > will definitely not need to be as big as the max payload size!),
> > either for the non-zero-copyable args or if the request's args aren't
> > page-backed. Unfortunately I think many servers may not be able to use
> > zero-copy due to the CAP_SYS_ADMIN requirements, so I think it'd be
> > ideal to minimize the memory consumption needed for the non-zero-copy
> > path, through the buffer pool for the payload buffers.
>
> Well, my problem is still why would we need to register ring entries if
> they don't have buffers - why not just register less ring entries then.

Entries may be used for servicing requests even if there are less
buffers than the number of entries. Most requests don't use the full
payload buffer size, so for example with incremental buffer
consumption, this would allow multiple entries to use non-overlapping
regions of the same buffer concurrently. As well, if we want to
support multiple buffer sizes per ring, there shouldn't be a 1:1
mapping between ents and buffers. There's also some requests that
don't need to use payload buffers at all (eg flush, fsync, rmdir,
etc).  As well for registered buffers / buffer pinning, it is more
optimal for the buffer pool to be allocated contiguously and pinned
contiguously rather than passed in one by one as a 1:1 entry:buffer
mapping.

Thanks,
Joanne

>
> At least I do not see the use case for ring entries without buffers.
> And that is exactly where my question about keeping the possibility for
> dynamic ring entry registration comes from. If ring entries register the
> buffers themselves, every ring could be set up with a single ring entry
> first and only once ring entries start to get used, libfuse could
> register more based on demand.
>
> Thanks,
> Bernd

> I think that is an argument for multi-size bufs per ring.

Which is what the buffer pool provides (decoupling buffers from
entries) if we choose to later add that in the future. With 1:1
entry:buffer mapping, each entry requires a buffer that supports the
max payload size at registration time.

>
> > consumption, this would allow multiple entries to use non-overlapping
> > regions of the same buffer concurrently. As well, if we want to
> > support multiple buffer sizes per ring, there shouldn't be a 1:1
> > mapping between ents and buffers. There's also some requests that
>
> I can counter argue that fuse-server can just send ring entries with a
> different payload sizes - fuse-server could dynamically create as many
> ring entries of the right size as needed.

I don't think this is a good idea. The server would have to
guesstimate this as it doesn't know the request size until it sees the
request. The server could speculatively pre-register ring ents ahead
of time but there's no guarantee that would be the right size for the
ring entry either. And once added, if the workload pattern changes,
then the ent and allocated buffer may not be useful anymore. This also
adds (imo needless) complexity to the kernel side. Additionally, the
dynamic ring ents would in the ideal case have payload buffers pinned,
which there is not an non-messy way to do (for buffer pools, the pool
buffer pinning goes through io-uring registered buffers infrastructure
prior to being passed to fuse at registration time). Imo the better
way if you wanted to dynamically add ents would be to add a new buffer
pool to the ring.

>
> > don't need to use payload buffers at all (eg flush, fsync, rmdir,
>
> Could be handled by multi buf sizes as well.

These requests don't require payload buffers.

This series already handles this case (eg not assigning a buffer from
the pool to the request).

>
> > etc).  As well for registered buffers / buffer pinning, it is more
> > optimal for the buffer pool to be allocated contiguously and pinned
> > contiguously rather than passed in one by one as a 1:1 entry:buffer
> > mapping.
>
> I don't think how the buffer is passed to fuse-client/kernel matters for
> (userspace) allocation. I had written before that libfuse's buffer
> allocation is not ideal, I had it continuous in initial development,
> then run into bug and for simplicity changed to per entry allocation.
> Switching back to larger allocations is simple.
> Regarding page mapping/pinning, why does it matter if the buffer in
> kernel is known as per entry or per allocation (assuming payload buffers
> are page aligned)?

It does matter how the buffers are passed to fuse for page pinning.
Please take a look at patch 8 ([PATCH v3 08/10] fuse: support
registered buffers as buffer pools in io-uring) [1]. The buffer pool
is contiguously allocated and registered as an io-uring registered
buffer, and then passed to fuse and by that point, the buffers have
already been pinned by io-uring.

Even irrespective of page pinning, it would still be good to pass the
buffers contiguously up front as a pool instead of per-ent, as the
purpose of this series is to decouple the 1:1 entry:buffer mapping. Eg
if the request an ent is servicing does not require buffers, a buffer
should not be reserved for that ent, amongst other benefits of a
decoupled mapping.

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260522205823.1597313-9-joannelkoong@gmail.com/

>
> I think the more important part for buf rings are pinned headers. For 4K
> pages the loss is maybe still acceptable and the remaining size could be
> used for small requests and I guess would have a perf benefit. For 64K
> pages the loss is quite a lot.

That is orthogonal to this series. This series focuses on payload
buffers, and header pinning is not part of this series as
bufpools/bufrings do not need pinned headers, as it makes no
difference for performance. As mentioned in the previous message, when
I was working on a separate series for removing the dispatch overhead,
I implemented header pinning as part of that, as that is when header
pinning becomes actually useful.

>
>
> Maybe let's summarize
>
> Bufrings
> - Fixed size, allocated at startup, cannot be easily increased or decreased
> - Allows 0 size payloads (FUSE requests that only need the header)
>
> Entries with payload
> - Allows more flexible setup - fuse server _could_ (not implemented as
> of now) create entries based on incoming IO.
>
> Neither provides real multi-size buffers per ring.
>

I think perhaps there is a misunderstanding of the patches in this series.

This series implements buffer pools, which decouple the 1:1 mapping
between ents and buffers that currently exists. Right now, ents and
buffers are tightly coupled where each entry has its own dedicated
payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where each buffer
must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size.

This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool where the buffers
are not statically mapped to an ent at registration time. This
decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use
fuse-io-uring. It is the necessary stepping stone for future
optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption (allowing
multiple entries to use non-overlapping regions of the same buffer
concurrently) and supporting multi-size buffers per ring.

Thanks,
Joanne

> Bernd

It's not just about the memory savings. Fundamentally, I believe it's
the wrong abstraction to statically couple ents to buffers 1:1 as a
fixed mapping. Entries and buffers serve different purposes. Entries
handle request flow, buffers hold data. Statically coupling them 1:1
is inflexible, introduces unnecessary complexity, and imo has no added
benefit. This series decouples these two concepts. It allows
dynamically adding ents in the future as *generic* ents not tied to a
particular buffer size (eg the added ent can be used to service *any*
request size, not just the size of the buffer it's tied to). With
tying ents to buffers, the server is stuck with a static distribution
that can't adapt to workload changes.

> > dynamic ring ents would in the ideal case have payload buffers pinned,

> > way if you wanted to dynamically add ents would be to add a new buffer
> > pool to the ring.

Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author is addressing feedback on the buffer allocation mechanism in fuse io-uring, specifically the issue that most request types require less than the maximum payload size, leading to inefficient memory usage. The author proposes decoupling entries from payload buffers and using a pool of dynamically selected buffers instead. They are seeking input on this approach.
Show original comment 
On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 1:59 PM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> This series adds buffer pools (implemented internally as a buffer ring) and
> zero-copy to fuse io-uring.
>
> Prior to this series, ents and buffers are tightly coupled where each entry
> has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where
> each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size. This
> is suboptimal as most request types require vastly less bytes than the maximum
> payload size and some requests do not require payload buffers at all.
>
> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool (internally managed as a
> buffer ring) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. This
> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring.
> It allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption and
> supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring.
>
> This series also allows servers to use io-uring registered buffers as the
> buffer pool which eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and
> translating virtual addreses per i/o request.
>
> This series additionally adds zero copy to fuse io-uring. The server can
> directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through
> an intermediary buffer. This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and using
> registered buffers.
>
> This series has a dependency on io-uring registered bvec buffers changes
> in [1].
>
> The throughput improvements from registered buffers and zero-copy depends on
> how much of the server's per-request latency is spent on data copying vs
> backing I/O. When backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible
> fraction of overall latency. Please also note that for the server to
> read/write into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
> as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
> server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
> overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
> from eliminating the memcpy.
>
> In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
> system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput improvement
> for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4% improvement for direct
> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s), a 8% improvement for buffered
> randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s), and a 6% improvement for buffered
> sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2670 MiB/s).
>
> Zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
> randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
> buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
> for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s). I didn't see
> enough of a clear improvement for writes due to write latency being I/O
> dominated.
>
> The benchmarks were run using:
>   fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
>   --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1
>
> The libfuse changes can be found in [2]. To test the server, run:
> sudo ~/libfuse/build/example/passthrough_hp ~/src ~/mounts/tmp
> --nopassthrough -o io_uring_zero_copy -o io_uring_q_depth=8
> Once this series is merged, the libfuse changes will be tidied up and
> submitted upstream.
>
> Thanks,
> Joanne
>
> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/io-uring/20260402160929.2749744-1-joannelkoong@gmail.com/T/#t
> [2] https://github.com/joannekoong/libfuse/commits/zero_copy_v3/

Miklos,

This series has stalled on an architectural question and I'd
appreciate your input on the direction.

Background:
In fuse io-uring, entries are reusable communication slots between the
kernel and the daemon. When a request needs to be sent from the kernel
to the daemon, it is assigned to an available entry. The entry carries
the request to userspace, and the response comes back through the same
entry. The number of entries determines how many requests can be
serviced concurrently. Currently, when the daemon registers an entry
during fuse io-uring setup, it passes in a payload buffer (sized to
the maximum payload, typically 1 MiB) alongside it. The buffer is tied
to the entry for the lifetime of the connection.

Problem that needs to be solved:
Most request types (lookup, getattr, open, etc) require far less than
the maximum payload size, and some (flush, fsync, etc) require no
payload buffer at all. Every entry reserves a full 1 MiB buffer
regardless of actual need. On systems with many entries, this memory
cost scales linearly - for example, 64 entries across 8 queues
requires 512 MiB of payload buffers alone.

Approach A:
Decouple entries from payload buffers. Entries become generic - any
entry can use any buffer from the pool. At setup time, the daemon
passes in a contiguous pool of memory, and the kernel dynamically
selects and recycles buffers from this pool as entries are assigned
requests. Entries servicing requests with no payloads consume no
buffer, and the pool can have fewer buffers than entries. This
supports adding multi-size buffers and incremental buffer consumption
(multiple entries using non-overlapping regions of the same buffer
concurrently) as optimizations in the future.

Approach B:
Keep the 1:1 entry-buffer coupling, but allow entries to be registered
with different payload buffer sizes at setup time. The daemon would
register entries with smaller buffers for metadata-heavy workloads and
larger buffers for data-heavy ones. This keeps the existing model of
passing in a payload buffer during entry registration at setup time
and requires no uapi additions. Each entry registration adds buffer
capacity whereas with buffer pools, buffer capacity would have to be
added as a separate pool.

I want to make sure I'm representing both approaches fairly - Bernd,
please correct anything I've gotten wrong.

Miklos, what are your thoughts?

Thanks,
Joanne
View on lore ↗
Signals: seeking_input, explaining_approach
Bernd Schubert Reviewed-by Neutral LLM
Bernd Schubert questioned whether header pinning is still possible, noting that if it's not done, io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() cannot be avoided, resulting in a context switch for sync requests. He also proposed an alternative approach where the payload would be registered per ring entry and only the header would become a buffer ring.

short question, I didn't check the code is header pinning still
*possible*? The problem if we do not pin headers is that
io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() cannot be avoided, as the application
cannot write into fuse-server buffers and headers without pre-pinned
pages. And io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() basically means a context
switch for all sync requests.

And that is exactly where my question about keeping the possibility for
dynamic ring entry registration comes from. If ring entries register the
buffers themselves, every ring could be set up with a single ring entry
first and only once ring entries start to get used, libfuse could
register more based on demand.

Thanks,
Bernd

Entries with payload
