Signals: question, alternative proposal
Bernd Schubert Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Bernd Schubert noted that the current implementation ties each entry in fuse io-uring to a full 1 MiB payload buffer, regardless of actual need, resulting in excessive memory usage on systems with many entries. He proposed decoupling entries from payload buffers as an approach to solve this problem.
Show original comment 
On 5/29/26 18:32, Joanne Koong wrote:
> On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 1:59\u202fPM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>>
>> This series adds buffer pools (implemented internally as a buffer ring) and
>> zero-copy to fuse io-uring.
>>
>> Prior to this series, ents and buffers are tightly coupled where each entry
>> has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where
>> each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size. This
>> is suboptimal as most request types require vastly less bytes than the maximum
>> payload size and some requests do not require payload buffers at all.
>>
>> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool (internally managed as a
>> buffer ring) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. This
>> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring.
>> It allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption and
>> supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring.
>>
>> This series also allows servers to use io-uring registered buffers as the
>> buffer pool which eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and
>> translating virtual addreses per i/o request.
>>
>> This series additionally adds zero copy to fuse io-uring. The server can
>> directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through
>> an intermediary buffer. This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and using
>> registered buffers.
>>
>> This series has a dependency on io-uring registered bvec buffers changes
>> in [1].
>>
>> The throughput improvements from registered buffers and zero-copy depends on
>> how much of the server's per-request latency is spent on data copying vs
>> backing I/O. When backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible
>> fraction of overall latency. Please also note that for the server to
>> read/write into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
>> as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
>> server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
>> overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
>> from eliminating the memcpy.
>>
>> In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
>> system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput improvement
>> for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4% improvement for direct
>> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s), a 8% improvement for buffered
>> randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s), and a 6% improvement for buffered
>> sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2670 MiB/s).
>>
>> Zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
>> randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
>> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
>> buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
>> for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s). I didn't see
>> enough of a clear improvement for writes due to write latency being I/O
>> dominated.
>>
>> The benchmarks were run using:
>>   fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
>>   --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1
>>
>> The libfuse changes can be found in [2]. To test the server, run:
>> sudo ~/libfuse/build/example/passthrough_hp ~/src ~/mounts/tmp
>> --nopassthrough -o io_uring_zero_copy -o io_uring_q_depth=8
>> Once this series is merged, the libfuse changes will be tidied up and
>> submitted upstream.
>>
>> Thanks,
>> Joanne
>>
>> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/io-uring/20260402160929.2749744-1-joannelkoong@gmail.com/T/#t
>> [2] https://github.com/joannekoong/libfuse/commits/zero_copy_v3/
>>
> 
> Miklos,
> 
> This series has stalled on an architectural question and I'd
> appreciate your input on the direction.
> 
> Background:
> In fuse io-uring, entries are reusable communication slots between the
> kernel and the daemon. When a request needs to be sent from the kernel
> to the daemon, it is assigned to an available entry. The entry carries
> the request to userspace, and the response comes back through the same
> entry. The number of entries determines how many requests can be
> serviced concurrently. Currently, when the daemon registers an entry
> during fuse io-uring setup, it passes in a payload buffer (sized to
> the maximum payload, typically 1 MiB) alongside it. The buffer is tied
> to the entry for the lifetime of the connection.
> 
> Problem that needs to be solved:
> Most request types (lookup, getattr, open, etc) require far less than
> the maximum payload size, and some (flush, fsync, etc) require no
> payload buffer at all. Every entry reserves a full 1 MiB buffer
> regardless of actual need. On systems with many entries, this memory
> cost scales linearly - for example, 64 entries across 8 queues
> requires 512 MiB of payload buffers alone.
> 
> Approach A:
> Decouple entries from payload buffers. Entries become generic - any
> entry can use any buffer from the pool. At setup time, the daemon
> passes in a contiguous pool of memory, and the kernel dynamically
> selects and recycles buffers from this pool as entries are assigned
> requests. Entries servicing requests with no payloads consume no
> buffer, and the pool can have fewer buffers than entries. This
> supports adding multi-size buffers and incremental buffer consumption
> (multiple entries using non-overlapping regions of the same buffer
> concurrently) as optimizations in the future.
> 
> Approach B:
> Keep the 1:1 entry-buffer coupling, but allow entries to be registered
> with different payload buffer sizes at setup time. The daemon would
> register entries with smaller buffers for metadata-heavy workloads and
> larger buffers for data-heavy ones. This keeps the existing model of
> passing in a payload buffer during entry registration at setup time
> and requires no uapi additions. Each entry registration adds buffer
> capacity whereas with buffer pools, buffer capacity would have to be
> added as a separate pool.
> 
> I want to make sure I'm representing both approaches fairly - Bernd,
> please correct anything I've gotten wrong.

I think for B "at setup time" is not entirely correct - buffers in the
current model buffers and entries can be registered at run time.


I have been thinking about approach C, which I think is in between A and B.
I my mind approach C does the registration per entry, but payload
buffers get added to a buf array, if a payload is set.
Max array size would be negotiated as in your approach or maybe better
with a config SQE. Different IO size could achieved by multiple buf arrays.


Thanks,
Bernd

Last comment 2026-06-04 [PATCH] iomap: avoid potential null folio->mapping deref during error reporting Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a potential null pointer dereference in the iomap error reporting path by ensuring that fserror_report_io() is called before decrementing read_bytes_pending, thus preventing a race condition where another in-flight read can complete and detach the folio.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants

On Wed, 03 Jun 2026 18:18:58 -0700, Joanne Koong wrote:
> When a buffered read fails, iomap_finish_folio_read() reports the error
> with fserror_report_io(folio->mapping->host, ...). This is called after
> ifs->read_bytes_pending has been decremented by the bytes attempted to
> be read.
> 
> For a folio split across multiple read completions, the folio is only
> guaranteed to stay locked while read_bytes_pending > 0. Once
> iomap_finish_folio_read() decrements read_bytes_pending, another
> in-flight read can complete and end the read on the folio, which unlocks
> it. This allows truncate logic to run and detach the folio (set
> folio->mapping to NULL). The error reporting path then can dereference a
> NULL folio->mapping. As reported by Sam Sun, this is the race that can
> occur:
> 
> [...]

Last comment 2026-06-01 Re: [PATCH v3 00/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds support for io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy operations to the FUSE (Filesystem in Userspace) kernel module, allowing for more efficient file system operations by decoupling entries from buffers and passing them as a contiguous pool upfront.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Bernd Schubert ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Bernd Schubert agrees with decoupling entries from buffers, but expresses disagreement on how buffers are passed in, preferring a contiguous pool upfront over individual buffer passing. Bernd Schubert expressed concerns that the contiguous buffer pool approach would make it difficult to implement dynamic registration and unregistering of buffers in libfuse, as it wouldn't be clear how to register on demand and unregistering would be even harder. He also noted that if races during registration are present, the current scheme's dynamic registration from the kernel perspective wouldn't change anything. reviewer reminded that Miklos requested dynamic registration of buffers, which was a change from the initial static approach taken from ublk tag model
Show original comment 
Well let's say, I don't see what speaks against decoupling it. The
memory saving effect is due to the special form of different request
sizes (0 and max payload).

---

My issue with this approach is still the same, incrementally registering
memory when one needs it is hard. Example, I have a few external SSDs
that are connected most of the time, but I only need them every few
days. Why would I need to spend memory for the fuse transport all the
time? The ideal situation would be: libfuse detects heavy usage and
registers more entries and buffers.
Current fuse kernel supports it. I just didn't get to it to implement
dynamic registration in libfuse yet - a matter of time.
Missing is some form of kernel unregister, although easy to add, if
unregister is treated as request OP.

With a contiguous buffer pool I wouldn't know how to register on demand
and unregistering is even harder.

Also, if we still have races during register phase, doing it on demand
wouldn't change anything in the current scheme - either the race is
there or it is not, because current scheme is dynamic registration from
kernel point of view.


No we need to keep that old scheme one way or the other, but we could
deprecate one form and avoid adding new features (like different buffer
sizes). However, if buffer pools have a limited usability, once needs to
do those features for both approaches.

---

Just a short reminder that it was actually Miklos to request the dynamic
registration way. Initially it was all static, taken from the ublk tag
model. Making it dynamic added some headache, but in the end I also
noticed that it can be useful.

buffer pool within one queue or alternatively, register/unregister a
new queue, depending on the workload. I think the details can be
worked out once we agree on the core design, but I don't think it
would be more complicated with contiguous buffer pools.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series, version 5, aims to switch the THP (Transparent Huge Pages) shrinker to use list_lru instead of the current implementation. The changes include fixing a long-standing issue in set_shrinker_bit() where the bit can be set on a dying child memcg instead of the ancestor that received the item. Other changes include adapting to the new lock_list_lru_of_memcg() signature, propagating folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() failure, and fixing a use-after-free bug in deferred_split_isolate(). The patches have been rebased onto the latest mm-unstable tree.

7 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
SeongJae Park Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer SeongJae Park noted that the patch causes a compilation error in UM mode due to an implicit function declaration of folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() when CONFIG_TRANSPARENT_HUGEPAGE is unset, and suggested defining the function for this case.
Show original comment 
Hi Johannes,

On Wed, 27 May 2026 16:45:16 -0400 Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org> wrote:

> The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
> queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
> on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
> can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
> 
>         alloc/unmap
>           deferred_split_folio()
>             list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
>             set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
> 
>         for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
>           mem_cgroup_iter()
>             shrink_slab(node, memcg)
>               shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
>                 if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>                   deferred_split_scan()
>                     walks memcg->split_queue
> 
> The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
> has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
> by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
> 
> list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
> streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
> widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
> queue as well.
> 
> The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
> object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
> created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
> 
> These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
> the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
> 
> Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
> Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
> Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>
> Reviewed-by: Lorenzo Stoakes (Oracle) <ljs@kernel.org>
> Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>

View on lore ↗
Signals: compiler_error, requested_change
Usama Arif Acked-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that the deferred split queue handles cgroups suboptimally by being per-NUMA node or per-cgroup instead of their intersection, which can lead to large pages being split on other nodes; requested conversion to list_lru, which sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists and streamlines reclaim walks.
Show original comment 
On 27/05/2026 21:45, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> When list_lru_add() races with cgroup deletion, the shrinker bit is set
> on the wrong group and lost. This can cause a shrinker run to miss the
> cgroup that actually has the object.
> 
> When the passed in memcg is dead, the function finds the first non-dead
> parent from the passed in memcg and adds the object there; but the
> shrinker bit is set on the memcg that was passed in.
> 
> This bug is as old as the shrinker bitmap itself.
> 
> Fix it by returning the "effective" memcg from the locking function, and
> have the caller use that.
> 
> Fixes: fae91d6d8be5 ("mm/list_lru.c: set bit in memcg shrinker bitmap on first list_lru item appearance")
> Reported-by: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>
> Reported-by: Sashiko
> Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>
> ---
>  mm/list_lru.c | 26 +++++++++++++++-----------
>  1 file changed, 15 insertions(+), 11 deletions(-)
> 

Acked-by: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>

On 27/05/2026 21:45, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
> queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
> on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
> can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
> 
>         alloc/unmap
>           deferred_split_folio()
>             list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
>             set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
> 
>         for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
>           mem_cgroup_iter()
>             shrink_slab(node, memcg)
>               shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
>                 if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>                   deferred_split_scan()
>                     walks memcg->split_queue
> 
> The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
> has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
> by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
> 
> list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
> streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
> widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
> queue as well.
> 
> The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
> object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
> created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
> 
> These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
> the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
> 
> Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
> Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
> Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>
> Reviewed-by: Lorenzo Stoakes (Oracle) <ljs@kernel.org>
> Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>

>>> queue as well.
>>>
>>> The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
>>> object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
>>> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
>>> created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
>>>
>>> These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
>>> the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
>>>
>>> Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>>> Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>>> Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>

Signals: requested changes
Johannes Weiner (author) Neutral LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the need to count MTHP statistics for sub-costly-order slab allocations in alloc_anon_folio(). They acknowledge that it's not an allocation expected to fail, and while they agree that fallback seems more correct, they don't think it matters and suggest leaving the code as is.

On Thu, May 28, 2026 at 02:32:06PM +0100, Usama Arif wrote:
> 
> 
> On 27/05/2026 21:45, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> > The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
> > queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
> > on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
> > can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
> > 
> >         alloc/unmap
> >           deferred_split_folio()
> >             list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
> >             set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
> > 
> >         for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
> >           mem_cgroup_iter()
> >             shrink_slab(node, memcg)
> >               shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
> >                 if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
> >                   deferred_split_scan()
> >                     walks memcg->split_queue
> > 
> > The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
> > has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
> > by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
> > 
> > list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
> > streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
> > widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
> > queue as well.
> > 
> > The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
> > object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
> > folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
> > created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
> > 
> > These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
> > the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
> > 
> > Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
> > Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>

> Ah sorry, should have caught this in the previous version, do we need
> 
> count_mthp_stat(order, MTHP_STAT_ANON_FAULT_FALLBACK);

This isn't an allocation we expect to fail with any sort of routine
that we'd need to capture it in the event counter. It would warn in
dmesg if it did. But in practice it can't happen at all, since it's a
sub-costly-order slab allocation and the allocator would loop and OOM
kill stuff until it succeeds.

> or maybe we just goto next instead of goto fallback and trty next
> viable order?

Again I don't think it matters, but fallback seems a bit more correct
because the size of the list_lru allocation doesn't change with lower
orders (until we hit 0).

So I think we can just leave it as is.

> __swap_cache_alloc() is also used by shmem direct swapin, so shmem can

>             folio_memcg_alloc_deferred()

branch due to "/* Direct swapin skipping swap cache & readahead */"

> @Baolin please correct me if I got it wrong :)
> 
> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() itself doesn't filter shmem out either; it
> only allocates the memcg list_lru metadata for deferred_split_lru:
> 
> int folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(struct folio *folio)
> {
> 	if (mem_cgroup_disabled())
> 		return 0;
> 	return folio_memcg_list_lru_alloc(folio, &deferred_split_lru, GFP_KERNEL);
> }
> 
> Since deferred_split_lru only queues anon large folios, doing this for
> shmem swapin doesn't buy us anything :)

Yes, agreed. I don't think it's a big deal / show stopper in terms of
user-visible effect, but of course still worth fixing.

On Wed, May 27, 2026 at 04:45:08PM -0400, Johannes Weiner wrote:
>When list_lru_add() races with cgroup deletion, the shrinker bit is set
>on the wrong group and lost. This can cause a shrinker run to miss the
>cgroup that actually has the object.
>
>When the passed in memcg is dead, the function finds the first non-dead
>parent from the passed in memcg and adds the object there; but the
>shrinker bit is set on the memcg that was passed in.
>

This means we just miss to reclaim some obj, but won't crash the kernel.

>This bug is as old as the shrinker bitmap itself.
>
>Fix it by returning the "effective" memcg from the locking function, and
>have the caller use that.
>
>Fixes: fae91d6d8be5 ("mm/list_lru.c: set bit in memcg shrinker bitmap on first list_lru item appearance")
>Reported-by: Usama Arif <usama.arif@linux.dev>
>Reported-by: Sashiko
>Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>

So we don't want to cc stable, right?

The fix looks right, so

Reviewed-by: Wei Yang <richard.weiyang@gmail.com>

-- 
Wei Yang
Help you, Help me

On Wed, May 27, 2026 at 04:45:16PM -0400, Johannes Weiner wrote:
>The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
>queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
>on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
>can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
>
>        alloc/unmap
>          deferred_split_folio()
>            list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
>            set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>
>        for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
>          mem_cgroup_iter()
>            shrink_slab(node, memcg)
>              shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
>                if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>                  deferred_split_scan()
>                    walks memcg->split_queue
>
>The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
>has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
>by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
>
>list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
>streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
>widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
>queue as well.
>
>The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
>object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
>folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
>created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
>
>These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
>the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
>
>Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>
>Reviewed-by: Lorenzo Stoakes (Oracle) <ljs@kernel.org>
>Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>
>---
> include/linux/huge_mm.h    |   7 +-
> include/linux/memcontrol.h |   4 -
> include/linux/mmzone.h     |  12 --
> mm/huge_memory.c           | 364 +++++++++++++------------------------
> mm/internal.h              |   2 +-
> mm/khugepaged.c            |   5 +
> mm/memcontrol.c            |  12 +-
> mm/memory.c                |   4 +
> mm/mm_init.c               |  15 --

[...]
>@@ -1379,6 +1285,14 @@ static struct folio *vma_alloc_anon_folio_pmd(struct vm_area_struct *vma,
> 		count_mthp_stat(order, MTHP_STAT_ANON_FAULT_FALLBACK_CHARGE);
> 		return NULL;
> 	}
>+
>+	if (folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(folio)) {
>+		folio_put(folio);
>+		count_vm_event(THP_FAULT_FALLBACK);
>+		count_mthp_stat(order, MTHP_STAT_ANON_FAULT_FALLBACK);
>+		return NULL;
>+	}

Nit: we have three possible failure point, and some duplicate
count_xxx_event/state().

Maybe we can have a followup cleanup for it.

Signals: suggested improvement, nit
Kairui Song Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Kairui Song noted that the deferred split queue handles cgroups suboptimally by not considering the intersection of nodes and cgroups, which can lead to large pages being split on other nodes; he suggested converting the deferred split queue to use list_lru, which properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists and streamlines list operations and reclaim walks.
Show original comment 
On Thu, May 28, 2026 at 4:48 AM Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org> wrote:
>
> The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
> queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
> on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
> can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
>
>         alloc/unmap
>           deferred_split_folio()
>             list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
>             set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>
>         for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
>           mem_cgroup_iter()
>             shrink_slab(node, memcg)
>               shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
>                 if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>                   deferred_split_scan()
>                     walks memcg->split_queue
>
> The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
> has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
> by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
>
> list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
> streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
> widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
> queue as well.
>
> The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
> object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
> created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
>
> These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
> the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.

Lance Yang noted that the new list_lru deferred split queue may lose reclaim in the cgroup.memory=nokmem case, and requested further investigation.

Hi Johannes,

On Wed, May 27, 2026 at 04:45:07PM -0400, Johannes Weiner wrote:
>This is version 5 of switching the THP shrinker to list_lru.
>
>Core of the new version is the list_lru/set_shrinker_bit fix up front,
>which minimally affects later patches; and a rebase onto the latest
>mm-unstable - replaced alloc_swap_folio() with __swap_cache_alloc().
>
>The changes seemed small enough that *I chose to keep the review tags
>from v4*. Please shout if you object to this!
>
>Changes in v5:
>- patch 1 is a new fix for a very old, pre-existing set_shrinker_bit()
>  problem in list_lru, where the bit can be set on a dying child memcg
>  instead of the ancestor that actually received the item. Pointed out
>  by Usama Arif and Sashiko; fix it first to make it minimally
>  backportable and so the conversion is safe.
>- patches 6 and 9 adapt to that fix's new memcg-by-reference
>  lock_list_lru_of_memcg() signature
>- collapse_huge_page(): propagate folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() failure
>  as SCAN_ALLOC_HUGE_PAGE_FAIL instead of leaking SCAN_SUCCEED and
>  falsely reporting a successful MADV_COLLAPSE (Usama Arif, Sashiko)
>- deferred_split_isolate(): fix a UAF by reading folio state before
>  list_lru_isolate(); once removed, a racing folio_put() frees the
>  folio via the lockless list_empty() check while we still touch its
>  flags and stats (Sashiko)
>- rebased to mm-unstable of 2026-05-27, which simplifies the flatten
>  prep patch (now anon-only, as alloc_swap_folio() was folded into the
>  new __swap_cache_alloc()) and moves the swap-side
>  folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() hook into __swap_cache_alloc(). Kairui,
>  I would appreciate an eyeball on that.
>
>Changes in v4:
>- guard folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() with mem_cgroup_disabled() to fix
>  NULL deref in __memcg_list_lru_alloc() when booting with
>  cgroup_disable=memory (e.g., kdump capture kernel) -- reported and
>  tested by Mikhail Zaslonko on s390 and x86
>- flatten if (folio) branches in alloc_swap_folio() and alloc_anon_folio()
>  in a prep patch so the list_lru allocation additions are a clean minimal
>  diff (Lorenzo)
>- folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() moved out of alloc_charge_folio() into the
>  anon-only collapse_huge_page() path; collapse_file() shares that helper
>  but its pages don't go on the THP shrinker queue (David)
>- guard folio_memcg_alloc_deferred() with order > 1; mTHPs below order-2
>  can't be queued on the deferred split list (David)
>- make deferred_split_lru static, hide behind folio_memcg_alloc_deferred()
>  wrapper with GFP_KERNEL (Lorenzo)

>- list_lru_lock_irq()/unlock_irq()/add_irq() irq-disabling variants;
>  use list_lru_add_irq() in deferred_split_scan() (Lorenzo)
>- reorder shrinker_free() before list_lru_destroy() (Lorenzo)
>
>Changes in v3:
>- dedicated lockdep_key for irqsafe deferred_split_lru.lock (syzbot)
>- conditional list_lru ops in __folio_freeze_and_split_unmapped() (syzbot)
>- annotate runs of inscrutable false, NULL, false function arguments (David)
>- rename to folio_memcg_list_lru_alloc() (David)
>
>Changes in v2:
>- explicit rcu_read_lock() in __folio_freeze_and_split_unmapped() (Usama)
>- split out list_lru prep bits (Dave)
>
>The open-coded deferred split queue has issues. It's not NUMA-aware
>(when cgroup is enabled), and it's more complicated in the callsites
>interacting with it. Switching to list_lru fixes the NUMA problem and
>streamlines things. It also simplifies planned shrinker work.
>
>Patch 1 fixes a pre-existing list_lru bug where the shrinker bit is
>set on the caller's memcg rather than the ancestor whose sublist the
>item actually lands on after a walk-up. Standalone, backportable; the
>rest of the series depends on it.
>
>Patches 2-5 are cleanups and small refactors in list_lru code. They're
>basically independent, but make the THP shrinker conversion easier.
>
>Patch 6 extends the list_lru API to allow the caller to control the
>locking scope. The THP shrinker has private state it needs to keep
>synchronized with the LRU state.
>
>Patch 7 extends the list_lru API with a convenience helper to do
>list_lru head allocation (memcg_list_lru_alloc) when coming from a
>folio. Anon THPs are instantiated in several places, and with the
>folio reparenting patches pending, folio_memcg() access is now a more
>delicate dance. This avoids having to replicate that dance everywhere.
>
>Patch 8 flattens the alloc_anon_folio() retry loop so the next patch's
>list_lru hook lands as a clean addition rather than nested deep inside
>an if (folio) block.
>
>Patch 9 finally switches the deferred_split_queue to list_lru.

As the changelog above says, the old queue is per-memcg only, rather
than per-memcg-per-node. So reclaim on one node can still walk the whole
memcg queue and split underused THPs from other nodes in the same memcg.

But I think the new one can lose reclaim in the cgroup.memory=nokmem
case ...

With nokmem, the deferred shrinker can still run from memcg reclaim,
because it is SHRINKER_NONSLAB. But the list_lru is no longer per-memcg:

__list_lru_init() clears memcg_aware,

	if (mem_cgroup_kmem_disabled())
		memcg_aware = false;

so list_lru_from_memcg_idx() falls back to the shared node list:

static inline struct list_lru_one *
list_lru_from_memcg_idx(struct list_lru *lru, int nid, int idx)
{
	if (list_lru_memcg_aware(lru) && idx >= 0) {
[...]
	}
	return &lru->node[nid].lru;
}

That makes the shrinker bit unreliable. __list_lru_add() still sets the
bit on the memcg passed in, but only when the list goes from empty to
non-empty:

bool __list_lru_add(struct list_lru *lru, struct list_lru_one *l,
		    struct list_head *item, int nid,
		    struct mem_cgroup *memcg)
{
	if (list_empty(item)) {
[...]
		if (!l->nr_items++)
			set_shrinker_bit(memcg, nid, lru_shrinker_id(lru));
[...]
		return true;
	}
	return false;
}

If memcg A adds the first folio, A gets the bit. If memcg B later adds a
folio to the same shared list, B does not get a bit, because the list
was already non-empty.

So in the A-first/B-later case, reclaim from B may not call the deferred
shrinker at all. The shared list is scanned from memcg reclaim only if
reclaim runs from the memcg that has the bit, such as A here, or from
global reclaim :)

Anyway, only after the shared list is emptied does the next memcg to add
a folio get to be the one with the bit, IIUC :)

Hopefully I didn't miss somthing important ...

Cheers, Lance

only allocates the memcg list_lru metadata for deferred_split_lru:

int folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(struct folio *folio)

		return 0;

Since deferred_split_lru only queues anon large folios, doing this for
shmem swapin doesn't buy us anything :)

Re: [PATCH v5 9/9] mm: switch deferred split shrinker to list_lru Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-03 Re: [PATCH v5 0/9] mm: switch THP shrinker to list_lru Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch removes the SHRINKER_NONSLAB flag from the THP shrinker, which was causing it to fire twice during reclaim cycles when cgroup.memory=nokmem is set. The flag declared independence from kmem, but this is no longer true. As a result, the shrinker will now behave consistently with other list_lru-backed shrinkers under nokmem mode, being driven by global reclaim only and skipped from memcg-internal reclaim.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Lance Yang ↳ replying to Johannes Weiner Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Lance Yang questioned whether dropping SHRINKER_NONSLAB would introduce a new problem by eliminating memcg-driven deferred split reclaim under nokmem mode, and expressed uncertainty about the correct fix.
Show original comment 
Thanks for working on this!

Wondering if this fix trades one problem for another, though ...

Before this series, the deferred split shrinker had a real per-memcg
queue. Even with cgroup.memory=nokmem, memcg reclaim could still scan
that memcg's own deferred_split_queue:

memcg reclaim -> deferred split shrinker -> sc->memcg->deferred_split_queue

With the fix, nokmem + w/o SHRINKER_NONSLAB falls back to a
non-memcg-aware shrinker:

memcg reclaim -> skip deferred split shrinker

root/global reclaim -> deferred split shrinker -> shared list_lru

Is that expected? There woud be no memcg-driven deferred split reclaim
under nokmem, IIUC ...

Not sure what the right fix is, as I am not a memcg expert ...

Cheers, Lance

Ongoing Submitted 2026-05-19 Last comment 2026-06-01 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH] mm/compaction: cap compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch limits the value returned by compact_gap() to prevent over-reserving memory for compaction, which can lead to unnecessary reclaim and failed compaction suitability checks. The current implementation returns a gap that scales exponentially with order, but is bounded by COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX. By capping this value at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX, the patch aims to improve compaction success rates and reduce unnecessary reclaim on fragmented production hosts.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Vlastimil (SUSE) ↳ replying to JP (Meta) Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Vlastimil questioned whether capping compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX would lead to unnecessary reclamation due to genuine memory pressure, suggesting that processes may continuously allocate and reclaim without the gap Reviewer Vlastimil questioned whether the reduction in reclaim is due to increased allocation size or a shift to direct reclaim, requesting clarification on the actual cause. Reviewer Vlastimil expressed skepticism about the patch's A/B test results, questioning why the free memory would suddenly stabilize and improve significantly after capping compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX.
Show original comment

is sized to fit without the gap.

JP Kobryn (author) ↳ replying to Vlastimil (SUSE) Needs Work LLM
Author acknowledged that order-9 THP failures were waking up kswapd and identified compact_gap() as a contributing factor, but did not indicate plans for further revision. Author acknowledged that the patch's performance improvements are consistent across different kernel versions and builds, but did not address any specific technical concerns or provide a clear resolution to previous feedback. Author addresses Vlastimil's concern about the impact of capping compact_gap() on order-9 THP allocations, explaining that it allows compaction to take over sooner and start working to produce large size pages needed for THP. Author acknowledged that bursty network allocations can reserve many pageblocks as high atomic, which affects the patch's assumptions about THP-size pages becoming eligible for allocation. Author is addressing concerns about over-reserving compact_gap() space, proposes to refactor patch to make it reclaim-focused and use a separate function for non-reclaim users.
Show original comment 
It wasn't actual pressure, but the repetitive order-9 THP failures that were
waking up kswapd. I should make this more clear in the changelog. After
looking into why so much reclaim was occurring though, the compact gap stood
out since it dictates the target amount to reclaim.

---

6.13. Additional benchmarks were done using a recent mm-new build as well,
and they showed similar reductions in reclaim.

---

Specifically in the order-9 case, the reclaim target goes from 1024 to 32.
What the data shows is that capping the gap allows compaction to take over
sooner and start working to produce large size pages needed for THP. Whereas
in the pre-patch state, trying to reclaim the full 2x THP delays compaction.

---

This patch was tested in isolation, but also occurring was the case where
bursty net allocations reserve many pageblocks as high atomic. So as
THP-size pages become eligible, their blocks are reserved before being
allocated as THP.

---

I'm thinking I could reframe this patch as reclaim-focused and use
min(2UL << order, COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX) as a reclaim-only target, while
either leaving the other non-reclaim users of this function alone or
using the 2x form you suggest above. i.e. I can split this function
into a separate reclaim_compact_gap() and use the originally proposed cap.
Thoughts?

JP Kobryn (author) ↳ replying to Vlastimil (SUSE) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed a concern that compact_gap() is not actually used as the reclaim target in kswapd_shrink_node(), but rather as a threshold for abandoning high order reclaim, and explained how this affects the interaction between kswapd and kcompactd. Author acknowledged that kswapd can be woken up on a high order failure despite lower order availability, suggesting compact_gap() still matters for higher orders. Author acknowledged that their patch and another recent change are addressing different paths in memory compaction, confirmed that capping compact_gap() is still beneficial despite the other change. The author addressed a concern that capping compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX would cause compaction_suitable() to remain false in fragmented environments, leading to kswapd continuing to reclaim in high order mode. The author explained that by capping the gap, compaction becomes suitable sooner and kswapd can fall back to order-0 balancing earlier, allowing kcompactd to be woken up for the original high order request. Author acknowledges a concern about the impact of compact_gap() on compaction decisions, but suggests that addressing it in this patch would be a different direction from its original focus. Author agrees that capping compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX is a better approach than their previous attempt at finding middle ground and confirms that a 2x cap resulted in mixed results, including some cases where it was worse.

[PATCH v2] mm/compaction: cap compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch limits the compact_gap() function to COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX, which is a threshold that represents the maximum number of pages that can be scanned by the compaction free scanner. The current implementation over-reserves by 32x, leading to unnecessary reclaim attempts and failed suitability checks. By capping compact_gap(), the patch aims to improve kswapd's reclaim efficiency and reduce THP fault fallbacks. The changes are based on A/B testing results from production hosts, which show significant improvements in reclaim efficiency, per-compaction success rates, and THP allocation success rates.

Last comment 2026-06-02 Re: [PATCH] mm/compaction: cap compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch caps the compact_gap() function at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX to prevent kswapd from getting stuck in high-order reclaim mode when compaction is not suitable, leading to earlier fallback to order-0 balancing and waking up kcompactd for the original high-order request. This change addresses an issue where compaction_suitable() remains false due to the effective threshold being too low, causing kswapd to keep reclaiming in high-order mode. The patch aims to improve system performance by allowing compaction to become suitable sooner and reducing unnecessary high-order reclaim efforts.

Last comment 2026-06-03 Re: [PATCH] mm/compaction: cap compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the compact_gap() function in the Linux kernel's mm/compaction module to cap its output at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX, rather than allowing it to grow indefinitely. The original implementation of compact_gap() was causing issues with THP (Transparent Huge Pages) allocations and reclaim operations. By capping the gap, the patch aims to reduce unnecessary reclaim and improve system performance. Initial testing showed improvements in THP allocation rates, but further investigation revealed that globally capping the gap is a better approach than capping it at specific downgrade gates. The patch's author has provided detailed test results and analysis to support this conclusion.

Patches Submitted (12)
[PATCH 0/4] arm64: cross-CPU NMI via SDEI Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series adds a new mechanism to deliver NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) on arm64 CPUs, using the SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure) firmware interface. This is necessary because current mechanisms have limitations: pseudo-NMI has a high performance cost and is disabled in some systems, while FEAT_NMI is not available in deployed silicon. The new mechanism uses event 0 of the standard SDEI software-signalled events and allows interrupting CPUs with locally masked interrupts without affecting their hot path performance. This will enable features like hard-lockup detection, all-CPU backtraces, and crash dumps to work correctly even when pseudo-NMI is disabled.

[PATCH 1/4] firmware: arm_sdei: add SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL support Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new function called sdei_event_signal() to the Linux kernel's firmware/arm_sdei.c file. The function signals a software-signalled event (event 0) to a target processor, making it pending and delivering an NMI-like signal even if interrupts are masked on that processor. This is achieved through a thin wrapper over the SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL call, which takes no locks and is therefore safe to call from NMI or crash context. The patch also updates the include/linux/arm_sdei.h file to add a declaration for sdei_event_signal(), and adds a new constant to the include/uapi/linux/arm_sdei.h file to define the SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL function number.

[PATCH 2/4] drivers/firmware: add SDEI cross-CPU NMI service for arm64 Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a cross-CPU NMI service using the SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure) software-signalled event on arm64 architecture. It allows delivering an NMI-like event to an interrupt-masked CPU without the pseudo-NMI hot-path cost. The first user of this feature is arch_trigger_cpumask_backtrace(), which can now reach masked CPUs, and falls back to the IPI path when SDEI is absent. This patch introduces a new driver for SDEI NMI and updates several files to support it.

[PATCH 3/4] arm64: wire SDEI NMI into the hardlockup watchdog Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds support for the SDEI (System Description Event Interface) NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) to the hardlockup watchdog on arm64 architecture. It allows the framework to use the SDEI NMI as a backend when available, and falls back to a perf-NMI counter if not. The patch adds configuration options for ARM_SDEI_NMI and selects HAVE_HARDLOCKUP_DETECTOR_ARCH to enable this feature. A per-CPU hrtimer is used to check the heartbeat of its buddy CPU and signal an event on a stalled CPU, running watchdog_hardlockup_check() NMI-like.

[PATCH 4/4] arm64: route crash_smp_send_stop() last resort through SDEI Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds the SDEI (System Description Event Interface) as a last resort to stop CPUs in case of a crash on arm64 architecture. It first attempts to stop CPUs using normal IPI and pseudo-NMI IPI, but if those fail, it uses SDEI to send a cross-CPU NMI that can reach even wedged CPUs with interrupts masked. The SDEI handler captures the CPU's state before parking it, making it less recoverable than a normal stop. This approach is chosen because an SDEI-stopped CPU never completes its event and is thus less recoverable.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series addresses six pre-existing bugs in the userfaultfd and pagemap code, which were previously carried as part of a larger feature series. The issues include missing updates to hardware Dirty/Accessed bits, incorrect handling of gigantic hugetlb pages, self-deadlocks when scanning unpopulated hugetlb ranges, loss of uffd-wp markers during PMD permission downgrades, and out-of-bounds writes in the vma_flags_t structure. Each bug is fixed independently and applies to the mm-new code directly, with some patches also carrying Cc: stable@ for backporting. The approach taken involves correcting specific functions and data structures to ensure accurate and safe handling of userfaultfd and pagemap operations.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds support for tracking the working set of VM guest memory using userfaultfd, allowing a VMM to identify cold pages and evict them to slower storage. The approach uses a new registration mode (MODE_RWP) that marks tracked pages as inaccessible while keeping them resident, and delivers a UFFD_PAGEFAULT_FLAG_RWP message to the registered handler in sync mode or auto-resolves faults in async mode. This enables efficient memory management for VM guests by detecting which pages are still being accessed and evicting cold ones to slower storage.

11 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about the decoupling of protnone PTE infrastructure from NUMA balancing by introducing CONFIG_ARCH_HAS_PTE_PROTNONE and making it dependent on this new option, which is selected by six architectures that support protnone PTEs. The author confirmed that there is no functional change and the same set of architectures continues to have working protnone support.
Show original comment 
pte_protnone() and pmd_protnone() detect present-but-inaccessible page
table entries. This capability is useful beyond NUMA balancing -- for
example, userfaultfd working set tracking uses protnone PTEs to track
page access without unmapping pages.

Introduce CONFIG_ARCH_HAS_PTE_PROTNONE to decouple the protnone PTE
infrastructure from CONFIG_NUMA_BALANCING. The six architectures that
support protnone PTEs (x86_64, arm64, powerpc, s390, riscv, loongarch)
now select this option, and CONFIG_NUMA_BALANCING depends on it.

No functional change -- the same set of architectures continues to have
working protnone support, but the infrastructure is now available
independently of NUMA balancing.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta) <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Acked-by: SeongJae Park <sj@kernel.org>
Acked-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: no functional change, same set of architectures
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Positive LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the naming convention for userfaultfd tracking PTE bits, specifically that they should be renamed to reflect their new purpose as 'userfaultfd' rather than 'wp'. The author agrees with this change and has already made the necessary renames in the patch.
Show original comment 
The uffd-wp PTE bit is about to gain a second consumer: userfaultfd
RWP will use the same bit to mark access-tracking PTEs, distinct
from mprotect(PROT_NONE) or NUMA-hinting PTEs. WP vs RWP semantics
come from the VMA flag; the bit is just "uffd has claimed this
entry." Drop the "_wp" suffix from the arch-private bit macros so
they reflect that.

  x86:   _PAGE_BIT_UFFD_WP  -> _PAGE_BIT_UFFD
         _PAGE_UFFD_WP      -> _PAGE_UFFD
         _PAGE_SWP_UFFD_WP  -> _PAGE_SWP_UFFD
  arm64: PTE_UFFD_WP        -> PTE_UFFD
         PTE_SWP_UFFD_WP    -> PTE_SWP_UFFD
  riscv: _PAGE_UFFD_WP      -> _PAGE_UFFD
         _PAGE_SWP_UFFD_WP  -> _PAGE_SWP_UFFD

Pure mechanical rename -- no behavior change.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Reviewed-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: SeongJae Park <sj@kernel.org>

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: agreement, acknowledgment of feedback
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about the uffd VMA-flag helpers reading vma->vm_flags directly, and agreed to switch them to use the vma_test_any_mask() accessor instead, which is the going-forward API for accessing per-mode masks. The change does not introduce any functional changes as it reads the same bits from vma->vm_flags and the masks fold to the same code.
Show original comment 
The uffd VMA-flag helpers read vma->vm_flags directly. Now that
config-gated per-mode masks exist, switch them to the vma_flags_t
accessor vma_test_any_mask(), which is the going-forward API and keeps a
single place (the VMA_UFFD_* masks) that knows which modes are available
on the current build.

No functional change: vma_flags_t is in union with vm_flags, so the same
bits are read, and the masks fold to the same code the open-coded
vm_flags tests produced -- verified identical on gcc and clang, 32- and
64-bit.

Suggested-by: Lorenzo Stoakes <ljs@kernel.org>
Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-8

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a concern, agreed to switch
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Needs Work LLM
The author acknowledged that the swapoff path needs to drop the per-vswap spinlock before calling try_to_unmap(), agreed to restructure in v2.
Show original comment 
Preparatory patch for userfaultfd read-write protection (RWP). RWP
extends userfaultfd protection from plain write-protection (WP) to
full read-write protection: accesses to an RWP-protected range --
reads as well as writes -- trap through userfaultfd.

Reserve VM_UFFD_RWP, add the userfaultfd_rwp() and
userfaultfd_protected() helpers, and wire up the smaps "ur" entry and
the trace-flag table the rest of the series will use. The flag is
gated on CONFIG_USERFAULTFD_RWP, which is introduced together with the
UAPI in a later patch; until then VM_UFFD_RWP aliases VM_NONE and
every downstream check folds to dead code.

Nothing sets or queries the flag yet.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Reviewed-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: SeongJae Park <sj@kernel.org>

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about silent destruction or bypassing of per-PTE RWP state in three mm paths: khugepaged, rmap, and GUP. They extended each path to treat VM_UFFD_RWP the same as VM_UFFD_WP, ensuring that access-tracking markers are preserved.
Show original comment 
Three mm paths outside the fault handler gate on the uffd PTE bit
today: khugepaged (skip collapse on ranges carrying markers), rmap
(cap unmap batching), and GUP (force a fault through
gup_can_follow_protnone). Extend each to treat VM_UFFD_RWP the same
as VM_UFFD_WP; otherwise per-PTE RWP state is silently destroyed or
bypassed.

khugepaged: try_collapse_pte_mapped_thp() and
file_backed_vma_is_retractable() already refuse to collapse or
retract page tables on ranges carrying the uffd PTE bit. Broaden the
VMA predicate from userfaultfd_wp() to userfaultfd_protected() so
VM_UFFD_RWP ranges get the same protection. hpage_collapse_scan_pmd()
needs no change \u2014 its existing pte_uffd() check already catches an
RWP PTE because it carries the uffd bit.

rmap: folio_unmap_pte_batch() caps batching at 1 for VM_UFFD_RWP so
the restore path handles each PTE with its own marker.

GUP: gup_can_follow_protnone() forces a fault on VM_UFFD_RWP VMAs
regardless of FOLL_HONOR_NUMA_FAULT. RWP uses protnone as an
access-tracking marker, not for NUMA hinting, so any GUP \u2014 read or
write \u2014 must go through the userfaultfd fault path.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Acked-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>

 /*
  * Indicates whether GUP can follow a PROT_NONE mapped page, or whether
- * a (NUMA hinting) fault is required.
+ * a (NUMA hinting or userfaultfd RWP) fault is required.
  */
 static inline bool gup_can_follow_protnone(const struct vm_area_struct *vma,
 					   unsigned int flags)
 {
+	/*
+	 * VM_UFFD_RWP uses protnone as an access-tracking marker, not for
+	 * NUMA hinting. GUP must always take a fault so the access is
+	 * delivered to userfaultfd, regardless of FOLL_HONOR_NUMA_FAULT.
+	 *
+	 * Only do so while the VMA is accessible. If it has been made
+	 * inaccessible (e.g. mprotect(PROT_NONE)), fall through to the guard
+	 * below: forcing a fault there would loop, as handle_mm_fault() makes
+	 * no progress on protnone in an inaccessible VMA, and the access is
+	 * denied regardless of RWP anyway.
+	 */
+	if ((vma->vm_flags & VM_UFFD_RWP) && vma_is_accessible(vma))
+		return false;
+
 	/*
 	 * If callers don't want to honor NUMA hinting faults, no need to
 	 * determine if we would actually have to trigger a NUMA hinting fault.

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: author acknowledged a fix is needed, author agreed with the approach
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed the concern that the uffd-wp PTE bit's meaning was not clearly defined, and responded by renaming kernel-internal names to reflect its new dependency on the VMA flag (WP or RWP). The rename is purely mechanical and does not change behavior. No fix is planned for this issue.
Show original comment 
Userfaultfd RWP will reuse the uffd-wp PTE bit to mark access-tracking
PTEs, alongside the write-protected ones it already marks. The bit's
meaning now depends on the VMA flag (WP or RWP), not on its name.

Rename the kernel-internal names that describe the bit:

  - pte/pmd/huge_pte accessors (and swap variants)
  - pgtable_supports_uffd() capability query
  - SCAN_PTE_UFFD khugepaged enum

The ftrace string emitted by mm_khugepaged_scan_pmd for this enum is
kept as "pte_uffd_wp" so existing trace-based tooling keeps matching.

Pure mechanical rename -- no behavior change.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Reviewed-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: SeongJae Park <sj@kernel.org>

-- 
2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, no behavioral change
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Needs Work LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the change_protection() primitive used by userfaultfd RWP, specifically that it needs to drop the per-vswap spinlock before calling try_to_unmap(). The author acknowledges this issue and plans to restructure in v2.
Show original comment 
Preparatory patch. Add the change_protection() primitive that
userfaultfd RWP will use.

An RWP-protected PTE is PAGE_NONE with the uffd PTE bit set. The
PROT_NONE half makes the CPU fault on any access; the uffd bit
distinguishes an RWP fault from a plain mprotect(PROT_NONE) or NUMA
hinting fault. MM_CP_UFFD_WP and MM_CP_UFFD_RWP share the same PTE
bit, so the two cannot be used together on the same range.

Two new change_protection() flags:

  MM_CP_UFFD_RWP            install PAGE_NONE and set the uffd bit
  MM_CP_UFFD_RWP_RESOLVE    restore vma->vm_page_prot, clear the uffd bit

Both are wired through change_pte_range(), change_huge_pmd(), and
hugetlb_change_protection() so anon, shmem, THP, and hugetlb all
share the same semantics.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Reviewed-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>

Reviewed-by: SeongJae Park <sj@kernel.org>

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledges a fix is needed
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about re-registering an already-registered range, explaining that the call is rejected with -EBUSY to prevent stranding markers without a flag to describe them. The author confirmed that this behavior differs from older kernels, which silently replaced mode bits on re-registration.
Show original comment 
Add the userspace interface for read-write protection tracking:

  - UFFDIO_REGISTER_MODE_RWP      register a range for RWP tracking
  - UFFD_FEATURE_RWP              capability bit
  - UFFDIO_RWPROTECT              install / remove RWP on a range

Introduce CONFIG_USERFAULTFD_RWP, auto-selected on 64-bit kernels with
ARCH_HAS_PTE_PROTNONE and HAVE_ARCH_USERFAULTFD_WP. The symbol gates
VM_UFFD_RWP (previously aliased to VM_NONE) and the smaps/trace-flag
hooks added in the preparatory patches; without it the UAPI bits added
here have nothing to drive and would be unreachable.

Registration sets VM_UFFD_RWP on the VMA. Combining MODE_WP with
MODE_RWP is rejected because both modes claim the uffd PTE bit.

UFFDIO_RWPROTECT is the bidirectional counterpart of
UFFDIO_WRITEPROTECT:

  - MODE_RWP              change_protection() with MM_CP_UFFD_RWP
                          installs PAGE_NONE and sets the uffd bit on
                          present PTEs
  - !MODE_RWP             change_protection() with MM_CP_UFFD_RWP_RESOLVE
                          restores vma->vm_page_prot and clears the bit

userfaultfd_clear_vma() runs the same resolve pass on unregister so
RWP state cannot outlive the uffd.

Re-registering a range must not drop a mode that installs per-PTE
markers (WP or RWP); doing so returns -EBUSY. This also closes a
pre-existing window where re-registering without MODE_WP would strand
uffd-wp markers: before, those caused extra write-faults but were
otherwise benign; with RWP preservation in place, a subsequent
mprotect() on a VM_UFFD_RWP VMA would silently promote the stale
markers to RWP.

The feature is not yet advertised. UFFDIO_REGISTER_MODE_RWP,
UFFD_FEATURE_RWP, and _UFFDIO_RWPROTECT are intentionally absent from
UFFD_API_REGISTER_MODES, UFFD_API_FEATURES, and UFFD_API_RANGE_IOCTLS,
so UFFDIO_API masks them out and the register-mode validator rejects
the bit. The follow-up patch adds fault dispatch and exposes the UAPI.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Reviewed-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>

+.. note::
+
+   Re-registering an already-registered range must not drop any of the
+   modes that install per-PTE markers \u2014 currently
+   ``UFFDIO_REGISTER_MODE_WP`` and ``UFFDIO_REGISTER_MODE_RWP``. Doing
+   so would strand markers with no flag to describe them, so the call
+   is rejected with ``-EBUSY``; userspace must issue
+   ``UFFDIO_UNREGISTER`` first. This differs from older kernels, which
+   silently replaced the mode bits on re-registration.
+

+int mrwprotect_range(struct userfaultfd_ctx *ctx, unsigned long start,
+		     unsigned long len, bool enable_rwp)
+{
+	struct mm_struct *dst_mm = ctx->mm;
+	unsigned long end = start + len;
+	struct vm_area_struct *dst_vma;
+	unsigned int mm_cp_flags;
+	struct mmu_gather tlb;
+	bool found = false;
+	VMA_ITERATOR(vmi, dst_mm, start);
+
+	VM_WARN_ON_ONCE(start & ~PAGE_MASK);
+	VM_WARN_ON_ONCE(len & ~PAGE_MASK);
+	VM_WARN_ON_ONCE(start + len <= start);
+
+	guard(mmap_read_lock)(dst_mm);
+	guard(rwsem_read)(&ctx->map_changing_lock);
+
+	if (atomic_read(&ctx->mmap_changing))
+		return -EAGAIN;
+
+	if (enable_rwp)
+		mm_cp_flags = MM_CP_UFFD_RWP;
+	else
+		mm_cp_flags = MM_CP_UFFD_RWP_RESOLVE;
+
+	/*
+	 * Pre-scan the range: validate every spanned VMA before applying
+	 * any change_protection() so a partial failure cannot leave the
+	 * process with only a prefix of the range re-protected.
+	 */
+	for_each_vma_range(vmi, dst_vma, end) {
+		if (!userfaultfd_rwp(dst_vma))
+			return -ENOENT;
+
+		if (is_vm_hugetlb_page(dst_vma)) {
+			unsigned long page_mask;
+
+			page_mask = vma_kernel_pagesize(dst_vma) - 1;
+			if ((start & page_mask) || (len & page_mask))
+				return -EINVAL;
+		}
+		found = true;
+	}
+	if (!found)
+		return -ENOENT;
+
+	vma_iter_set(&vmi, start);
+	tlb_gather_mmu(&tlb, dst_mm);
+	for_each_vma_range(vmi, dst_vma, end) {
+		unsigned long vma_start = max(dst_vma->vm_start, start);
+		unsigned long vma_end = min(dst_vma->vm_end, end);
+		unsigned int flags = mm_cp_flags;
+
+		/*
+		 * On resolve, try to upgrade writability per-VMA --
+		 * MM_CP_TRY_CHANGE_WRITABLE WARNs in
+		 * maybe_change_pte_writable() if the VMA is not VM_WRITE,
+		 * and RWP can be registered on PROT_READ-only mappings.
+		 */
+		if (!enable_rwp && vma_wants_manual_pte_write_upgrade(dst_vma))
+			flags |= MM_CP_TRY_CHANGE_WRITABLE;
+
+		change_protection(&tlb, dst_vma, vma_start, vma_end, flags);
+	}
+	tlb_finish_mmu(&tlb);
+
+	return 0;
+}

+static int userfaultfd_rwprotect(struct userfaultfd_ctx *ctx,
+				 unsigned long arg)
+{
+	int ret;
+	struct uffdio_rwprotect uffdio_rwp;
+	struct userfaultfd_wake_range range;
+	bool mode_rwp, mode_dontwake;
+
+	if (atomic_read(&ctx->mmap_changing))
+		return -EAGAIN;
+
+	if (copy_from_user(&uffdio_rwp, (void __user *)arg,
+			   sizeof(uffdio_rwp)))
+		return -EFAULT;
+
+	ret = validate_range(ctx->mm, uffdio_rwp.range.start,
+			     uffdio_rwp.range.len);
+	if (ret)
+		return ret;
+
+	if (uffdio_rwp.mode & ~(UFFDIO_RWPROTECT_MODE_DONTWAKE |
+				UFFDIO_RWPROTECT_MODE_RWP))
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	mode_rwp = uffdio_rwp.mode & UFFDIO_RWPROTECT_MODE_RWP;
+	mode_dontwake = uffdio_rwp.mode & UFFDIO_RWPROTECT_MODE_DONTWAKE;
+
+	if (mode_rwp && mode_dontwake)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	if (mmget_not_zero(ctx->mm)) {
+		ret = mrwprotect_range(ctx, uffdio_rwp.range.start,
+				       uffdio_rwp.range.len, mode_rwp);
+		mmput(ctx->mm);
+	} else {
+		return -ESRCH;
+	}
+
+	if (ret)
+		return ret;
+
+	if (!mode_rwp && !mode_dontwake) {
+		range.start = uffdio_rwp.range.start;
+		range.len = uffdio_rwp.range.len;
+		wake_userfault(ctx, &range);
+	}
+	return ret;
+}

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Kiryl (Meta) (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the uffd PTE bit being dropped in various kernel paths that rewrite PTEs on VM_UFFD_RWP VMA, and promised to wire its preservation through all these paths.
Show original comment 
The uffd PTE bit must survive any kernel path that rewrites a PTE
on a VM_UFFD_RWP VMA, otherwise the marker that carries PAGE_NONE
semantics is silently dropped and the next access leaks past RWP
tracking. Wire the preservation through every path that rewrites a
VM_UFFD_RWP PTE.

Swap and device-exclusive: do_swap_page(), restore_exclusive_pte(),
and unuse_pte() (swapoff()) re-apply PAGE_NONE when the swap PTE
carries the uffd bit and the VMA has VM_UFFD_RWP.

Migration: remove_migration_pte() and remove_migration_pmd() do the
same after the migration entry is replaced with a real PTE/PMD.

Fork: __copy_present_ptes(), copy_present_page(), copy_nonpresent_pte(),
copy_huge_pmd(), copy_huge_non_present_pmd(), and
copy_hugetlb_page_range() keep the uffd bit on the child when the
destination VMA has VM_UFFD_RWP, matching the existing VM_UFFD_WP
handling. Add VM_UFFD_RWP to VM_COPY_ON_FORK so the flag itself
propagates.

mprotect(): change_pte_range() and change_huge_pmd() restore PAGE_NONE
after pte_modify()/pmd_modify() have recomputed the base protection
from a (possibly user-changed) vm_page_prot. pte_modify() preserves
_PAGE_UFFD, so the bit stays; we just have to force PAGE_NONE back
on top.

Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4-6
Acked-by: Mike Rapoport (Microsoft) <rppt@kernel.org>

[PATCH v4 0/3] x86/tdx: Fix port I/O handling bugs Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series fixes two bugs in the TDX guest port I/O #VE emulation and extracts a helper function from KVM to avoid open-coding partial-register-write logic. The first bug is an off-by-one error in the mask used to clip the I/O value, while the second bug involves incorrect zero-extension of 32-bit IN instructions. A new helper function is added to handle instruction assignment, which replaces the original code and improves maintainability.

[PATCH v4 1/3] x86/tdx: Fix off-by-one in port I/O handling Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes an off-by-one error in port I/O handling for the x86/tdx architecture. The issue was caused by a mask calculation that included one extra bit, leading to incorrect results for I/O operations of size 1 byte. The patch corrects this by subtracting 1 from the result of BITS_PER_BYTE * size in the GENMASK calculation.

[PATCH v4 2/3] x86/insn-eval: Add insn_assign_reg() helper Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new helper function called insn_assign_reg() to the x86 instruction evaluation module. The function writes a value into a register with x86 partial-register-write semantics, which means it handles different byte sizes and zero-extension correctly. The function is added to a header file so that both KVM's instruction emulator and TDX guest #VE handler can use it. The patch also rewrites the helper function using arithmetic instead of pointer punning to make it more portable. There is no functional change in this patch, which means it only adds a new helper function without changing any existing behavior.

[PATCH v4 3/3] x86/tdx: Fix zero-extension for 32-bit port I/O Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch fixes a bug in the x86/tdx module where 32-bit port I/O operations were not properly zero-extending their results to 64 bits, as required by the x86 architecture rules. The fix involves using the insn_assign_reg() function to write the result back into RAX with proper partial-register-write semantics. This patch addresses an issue introduced in a previous commit (x86/tdx: Port I/O: Add runtime hypercalls) and was reported by Borys Tsyrulnikov.
